“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at Sydney, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia's Nonparell
Illustrated Newspaper,
“The Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and dis
tinctive Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
One Person, one Vote.
Comnlete Secularisation and Freedom of State
Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
Auswaiia for the Australians.—The cheap
Chinaman, the cheap nigger, and the
cheap European pauper to be absolutely
excluded.
A State Bank, the issue of bank-notes to be a
State monopoly.
The direct election of Ministers by Parlia-
ment, instead of Party Government or
rather Government by Contradiction.
A new Parliamentary System - one House to
be elected by constituencies as at present;
the other to be chosen by the whole
country voting as one Constituency.
A Universal System of Compulsory Life
Insurance.
The entire Abolition of the Private Owner-
ship of Land.
The Referendum.
The Abolition of Titles of so-called “ nobility.”
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter which is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started eleven years ago with no capital but brains,
ar.d has become a vast property, because it pos-
sesses a vitality lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists
have unavailingly spent large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once bo seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to tlio fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception;
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress : it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maonland
Ranges. A.paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

SATURDAY, JUNE 25, 1892.
The Cause of Depression.
It has been represented to the Government that many
men at present without work are willing to take employ-
ment at such reasonable wages as may be offered. The
Government have reason to believe that many persons
would be willing to give employment to labourers at
rates below those usually paid at present, if they could
obtain labourers at such rates. The Government will,
therefore, be glad if any persons willing to give such em-
ployment will communicate with the officer in charge of
the Government Labour Bureau, stating the number of
men they are willing to employ, and the wages which they
are willing to pay. The Government confidently invite
this assistance from private citizens for the purpose of
dealing with the present difficulty. —Circular issued by
the Kanakiloriff Government of Queensland.
The working classes must be prepared to accept a lower
rate of wages before employment can be found. When-
ever this is done there will be no more unemployed.—
Speech by Sir Thomas MTlwkaitii in the Queensland
Assembly.
An impossible rate of wages has been fixed, whereas, if
n proper rate had been established, there would have been
no unemployed.— Speech by B. D. Morehead in the
Queensland Assembly.
A few days ago the Queensland Legislature
set itself to solemnly consider the problem
of the unemployed, and-except for the
dissent of a very few members of very
small monetary importance—it was unani-
mous in its decision. The cause of the
difficulty, so it decided, is the exorbitant
wages demanded by the working-classes of
the province. If they hadn’t exacted these
exorbitant wages in the past, there would
never have been any unemployed ; if
they will promise not to want them for
the future, there will never bo any
unemployed again. A thirst after high
wages is the origin of poverty, hunger, want
of work, and all the other social ills of the
day—therefore, it follows that in countries
where wages are low there is prosperity,
happiness, and general contentment, and in
countries where they are high there is woe,
misery, and general starvation. And from
this it further follows that a rise of wages is
the short road to wretchedness, while a re-
duction is the trunk-line to happiness, peace,
and tranquil contentment, and as one re-
duction of wages brings general prosperity it
may bo reasonably inferred that another
reduction will bring more prosperity, and by
the time wages are reduced to nothing at all,
the millennium will have arrived and Queens-
land will be a Paradise. Also, it is unani-
mously x’esolted by the Legislature at This-
bane that high wages are the only cause of
the prevailing depression, and that high
rents, high interest, inflated land values,
and about the biggest debt (per head) on
earth, incurred chiefly to build political rail-
ways for crooked politicians, have nothing
whatever to do with the trouble. From
which resolutions it may be fairly assumed
that the Queensland Legislature is either a
harmful lun di?, or else that it is the most
utterly mendacious political organisation by
which Australia is at present afflicted.
# # *
The assumption that wages must come
down whenever a period of scarcity arrives,
is one which has been accepted so long that
the world has almost ceased to question it.
Tn times of poverty wages are the first thing
that comes down ; in times of plenty they
are the last thing that goes up. They come
down fast and they go up again very slowly
—moreover, they come clown further, in the
one instance, than anything else, and in the
other they do not rise in anything like the
same proportion. Rent, interest, and wages
are the three great charges which absorb the
produce of the earth, and of these rent keeps
going up for ever and ever : while interest
remains about stationary, when an average
is struck all over the civilised globe, or else
exhibits a slight tendency downwards. Rent
and intei’est are the two first charges upon
production, and when they are satisfied the
balance, whatever it may be, goes in wages.
When times are good rent increases with
enormous rapidity ; it rises a hundred per
cent, while wages only rise twenty, and then
there is a bitter wail concerning the exoi’bi-
taut exactions of Labour, and the way in
which agitators and trades-unions are incit-
ing the masses to be a clog and a burden
upon the national industry. When times
are bad rent keeps on increasing, only more
slowly, and wages go down heavily, and then
a melancholy lamentation goes abroad to the
effect that the selfish and ignorant toiler has
extinguished the prosperity of the country
by his greed, and that it is necessary to take
steps to resist him. And when depres-
sion becomes utter stagnation, os is the
case at present with Queensland, rents
still go up, oidy more slowly yet, and
wages fall to the very lowest point at which
the worker can eke out a subsistence, and
callous speculators of the Griffith or M‘ll-
wraith type point out (while passing Bills
to flood the country with cheap niggers and
thus increase the fortunes of those who have
grabbed the rich sugar-lands) that the absurd
and outrageous demands of the working-
classes are the cause of the whole trouble.
There never was a period, however ruinous,
in the history of any Australian province,
when rents in the aggregate were not going
up, and as they keep on rising the margin
left for wages becomes, in each succeeding
period of depression, more and more pre-
carious.
# # #
It is a recognised axiom that as population
increases wages diminish. The human race,
in a dull, patient, lymphatic way, mostly
accepts the thing that is as the thing that
must be, and it therefore assumes that the
increase of population is itself the cause of
this decline. But in reality the increase of
population merely affords opportunity for
the increase of rent, and the unnatural
expansion of rent diminishes the amount
that is available for wages. There is no
sense or logic in the matter, save the logic
of hard, brutal swindling. The man who
inherited from his great-grandfather a scrap
of waste land which, without any effort on
his part, has now become the centre of a
great city, claims to receive in rent about
five hundred times as much as his ancestor
received, and his claim is allowed. The
man whose great-grandfather made shoes
for a certain weekly wage in a humpy on
that same piece of land in the early days is
at least equally entitled to receive live hun-
dred times as much for making shoes in the
same place, but his claim would be regarded
as an evidence of insanity. Yet, when care-
fully analysed, his case is much the better of
the two. It is population which creates
the value of land; if land could increase
in value without population then allotments
at the bottom of the Pacific would be as
valuable as those in Collins-street, Mel-
bourne. When a certain aggregation of
men, by their presence, have given value to
the land then they are charged an enormous
rent for having done so, and through this
rent wages diminish. If the increase of
wealth were evenly divided between wages
and rent, interest remaining stationary, then
rents would still have risen in Queensland,
and wages would be at least four times
greater than they were 30 or 40 years ago.
As it is, interest has fallen, wages have fallen
to a point that is closely akin to starvation,
and while the wealth of the country has
increased by, at least, £lOO per head during
the past generation, rent has swallowed
it all—and more. And even at this point
the Queensland landholders and land-
boomers are not satisfied, and therefore the
Government suggests, as a work of Inmccol-
ence, that employment should be found for
the poor at still lower wages than those now
prevailing !
# # *
This means inevitably an all-round reduc-
tion which will gradually affect every branch
of industry. Wages are always levelling down
the only thing that is always levelling up is
rent. When one employer hires, through
the Government labour-bureau, a number of
broken-spirited scrag-ends of humanity at
half-wages, every other employer must either
reduce his employes to the same level or else
discharge them and hire a similar squadron
of cheap “unemployed” to take their
places. If he does not he will be undersold
and gradually ruined, for the employer, as
well as the employed, suffers from the cease-
less upward movement of rents —except in
the comparatively few instances when he is
himself a landlord, and his profits mostly
come under the head of interest which is a
declining quantity. And when wages have
been subjected to a further aggregate reduc-
tion of perhaps 25 per cent, the depression,
as Sir Samuel Griffith prophesies, will
begin to fade away, and a boom will arise in
the sultry North. There will be a greater
demand for land ; sales will be more
frequent; better prices will be realised ;
dead estates with which all manner of grasp-
ing companies and syndicates have been ham-
pered for years will begin to be live assets
once more ; and rents will go up. Just now
the triple demands of rent, interest and
wages amount to more than the province
produces ; if a large percentage is taken off
wages it will probably leave a balance to the
good and rents will cheerfully rise and
absorb that balance. When rents rise fast it
is called prosperity. Possibly wages, having
previously gone down 25 per cent, in the
days of stagnation, will go up 10 per cent, in
the period of inflation, and this will be
alluded to as the grasping selfishness of the
working classes. And when the next period
of poverty arrives that 10 per cent, rise will
be set down as the cause of the renewed
trouble, and another scheme of general re-
duction will be announced. But there will
be no general reduction in rent; it will keep
rising as before.
# * *
The statements issued by MTlwkaith and
Morehead, to the effect that when wages
are reduced there will be no more “ unem-
ployed,” is so glaring a perversion that it
would be an insult to their intelligence to
suppose that they themselves believed it
true. If that theory is sound, then there are
no unemployed in London, where thousands
of workmen can be procured at the exceed-
ingly low rate of 15s. or so per week, and
where working women can be obtained in
still greater numbers for half that amount —
there is no submerged tenth—no “General”
Booth —no great colonisation scheme to get
rid of those millions whose wages have been
reduced over and over again, and who And
employment scarcer with each reduction.
Also, there is no Italian immigration pro-
blem, for the Italian, whose wages have been
reduced even lower than those of the
Englishman, has necessarily abundance of
work at home, and doesn’t want to be a
cheap blackleg in Queensland ; and there is
no Chinese difficulty, for the Chinaman’s
wages are down lower than those of the
Italian, and he has so much employment
and so much tranquil happiness in his
own flowery land that he can’t desire
to come to Australia at all. But the
reality, as everybody knows, is that the
reduction of wages only makes competition
keener, and the battle of life harder, and the
army of the unemployed more and more
numerous. Moreover, the assertion that but
for the exorbitant demands of Labour there
would be no superfluous poor in Queensland
reads like a blushless lie when uttered by
the same politicians who deliberately im-
ported these same poor—who brought
in paupers by thousands and tens of thou-
sands, and who are bringing in more and
still more paupers to this day, despite the
alleged stoppage of the immigration policy.
But even the batch of houseless wretches who
were imported with the public money and
dumped on the hungry shores of Queensland
a week or two ago will do something for the
province. Some of them, most likely, will
die of want, and call down curses on Griffith
and MTlwraith and the affable B. 1),
Morehead from their graves, but the rest
will serve to increase the population of the
country, and, through their competition,
wages will fall, and through the falling of
ivages rents will rise. And to make rents
rise is the last and noblest aim of modern
civilisation. For there is only one civilisa-
tion, and it consists principally of land
booms, and the Kanakilorifl is its prophet.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Melbourne Tramway Scare.
Two years ago the one-pound shares of the
Melbourne Tramway and Omnibus Co., with
ten shillings paid-up, were sold as high as T9,
and quoted up to T9 8s. On Saturday last,
after a long downward progression, the
announcement that the directors would have to
omit the expected July dividend sent them
down to 16s. at one pop -the fall being Is. 6d.
on the price of the previous day—and they
finished at 16s. 6d. No one can tell where they
will be by tire end of the month since it is now
remembered that the cables will have to be
completely renewed within a. year or so, a
contingency which may necessitate a call, and
hat in three years the Legislature will have the
right to reduce the fares, a measure upon which
public opinion will assuredly insist. It is quite
evident that the wires are being pulled just
as dexterously now to induce shareholders
to sell on a panic as they were pulled
three years back to tempt the public
to buy at any price. A similar game was
played to the great advantage of the inner
ring when the mines on the famous Comstock
Lode in Nevada were carefully boomed from
eight dollars up to eight hundred and then just
as artistically pulled down again to something
closely approaching the original figure. So far
as the Melbourne trains are concerned, there is
no doubt that the boom and the burst-up have
both been ■engineered with very great skill, and
very probably those who planned the first are
not entirely unconcerned in the second. No-
thing can be nicer than to buy shares at 10s.,
sell them at £9, and then frighten the holder
into letting you have them again at the original
figure. Such an operation may be repeated
five or six times in the 23 years the Melbourne
Tramway Company’s monopoly has to last, and
the lucky participators in the profits thereto
will find themselves and their families very well
provided for at the end of the period. Mean-
while, looking at the rush for shares in 1890
and the rush to get rid of them in 1892, the
only conceivable comment must be couched in
the phraseology of Bumble— “ The public is a
bass.” But that conclusion does not affect the
fact that the Tramway ring contains the most
audacious and unscrupulous schemers in Aus-
tralia.

Gladstone on Freedom.
The old Liberal Party in England is fast getting
out of date. Except on the one question of
Irish autonomy it is but little in advance of
the despised Tory gang with its policy of stag-
nation and stupidity. It has a grovelling rever-
ence for “ freedom of contract” and “vested
interests ” and all the old, sacred maunderiugs
of British politics. It grieves over poverty and
suffering, but it won’t interfere with them lest
it should break up the glorious liberty of the
British citizen. It won’t tax land because that
would burst the Constitution. It won’t inter-
fere with the “ rights ” of the land-grabber or
the sweater or the usurer because they are all
Britons, and Britons never, never shall be.
slaves. Even Gladstone, one of the most
advanced of the ancient Liberals, drivels like
the rest when brought face to face with a
tremendous social evil, as witness his recent
answer to the London Trades Council deputa-
tion re the eight-hours question t
Mr. Gladstone said, in the course of the conference,
that lie \Vas not sanguine of the eight-hour system being-
legalised. The difficulty Involved in such a course was
that it iCould destroy freedom.
So it would. It would destroy the freedom of
the British railway employe, who is now at
liberty to work 16 hours a day for seven days
a week. It would be an unjustifiable inter-
ference with the ’bus-driver, who sits on his box
for 17 hours per diem. It would create a bloated
and unconstitutional tyranny, to wrest from the
sweated seamstress her hitherto unalienable
privilege of toiling from eight a.m. till midnight,
or thereabouts. It would wreck the inborn
right, secured by Magna Gharta, of any man to
do two men’s work, and thus leave another man
unemployed. The fetish of the Gladstoniail
party is Liberty. In England to-day every
man and woman is at liberty to accept work
at any hours or wages he or she may think
best, or to refuse it if the hours and wages don’t
suit; and every employer is free to offer any
terms he may consider best, and if the terms
are unjust the employe can decline them with the
pride of a free citizen. Also, every landlord is
free to demand any rent he likes, and the tenant
isn’t compelled to close with his terms Unless he
is so inclined,' and all over the glorious isles of
Britain every person, however humble, is free.
The Liberal party grovels before this fetish of
British freedom with a great grovel, and Glad-
stone drivels about it in his old age as a thing
that he desires to leave behind him untarnished
—that it may be a precious legacy to succeeding
generations. And in exactly the same manner
the Briton who falls overboard in the middle
of the Atlantic is free to swim ashore—but all
the same he never gets there; and the man who is
marooned on an ice-floe is at liberty not to
starve or to freeze, but he freezes all the same.
Also, Gladstone himself is at liberty to live
for ever, but the chances are that he will die
none the less. The freedom which he is so
anxious to save from destruction is only the
freedom of every Englishman to accept work at
inhuman hours and starvation wages, or die
miserably in the gutter—to pay an exorbitant
rent to a hereditary land-stealer or be crowded
into the sea. It is a pitiable spectacle to see a
statesman of Gladstone’s reputation maunder-
ing over the preservation of individual liberty,
when this is all it amounts to. Liberty, of the
good old constitutional kind, has been the curse
of England, and it would have been better for
that unhappy land that its fetish had never
been invented.

A Scalp and Shinbones.
The Maoriland Government wants a reliable
guarantee from General Booth that, if his
paupers are permitted to locate themselves in
the country, none of them shall ever become
chargeable on the benevolent institutions of the
province. Booth declines to give this guarantee.
As that bilious prophet wants to have the
authority of a landlord over his Salvation
colony it naturally follows that he would have
power to eject any colonist who didn’t pay his
rent, and that evicted individual would, almost
to a certainty, end by becoming a pauper
chargeable, not to Booth, but to the Maoriland
revenue. Should the B.Vllanc'E Government
allow Booth to bring his moral wrecks to
Maoriland the shortest way out of the difficulty
is to absolutely prohibit ejectment, but to allow
the General, for the sake of peace and order, to
execute any Salvation emigrant who proves
irreclaimable, and send his scalp and shinbones
to the Treasury as a guarantee that he won’t
become a burden on the country. These
remains could bo accompanied by a certificate
stating the reasons which induced Booth to
take this extreme course. Nothing short of
this, so far as we can see, will meet the case.
If the Salvation potentate isn’t allowed to eject
his paupers they may simply live rent-free on
the property, and raise Cain generally, and do
no work. If he is allowed to eject them he
will merely shift the burden on to someone
else. He might, of course, be allowed to flog
the Salvationists and set them to hard labour on
the big drum, but this course would involve
too much trouble. Execution is the only remedy,
after all—execution, and plenty of it, and no
questions asked.


An “Enormous Shrinkage of Assets” has
occurred to the Hon. James Munro’s hurst-up
bank since that eminent financier got away to
New Babylon. A few figures will serve to
indicate the length, breadth and depth of the
business capacity which qualified Munro for
the positions of Premier and Agent-General of
Victoria. In August last Munro, as chairman
of the Real Estate, &c., Bank, told the share-
holders that their property was in such a healthy
condition that no further call of capital was
contemplated, and the directors cherished hopes
of being able to recommence business shortly.
Everything was as it professed to be on the
balance-sheet, and the financial outlook was
clear and beautiful. So said James. The
liquidators’ report on these same assets leaves
the shareholders at liberty to say whether the
vanished chairman was a liar or a fool, or both.
They have dropped their “ sugar ” and can take
their choice. The properties described as in
splendid condition last August are now “written
down” by the liquidators to the tune of
£785,000. Bills receivable have melted from
£IIO,OOO to £29,000. Overdrawn accounts
representing £99,000 in the books are estimated
to produce £43,000—a “shrinkage” which
could be best explained by a published list of
the over-drawers. Finally, the liquidators have
achieved a brilliant stroke of satire by “ cancel-
ling ” a reserve fund of £IOO,OOO, together with
certain mystic “ undivided profits ” amounting
to £32,000. Considering that rather more than
a million sterling of Munro’s visionary assets
are cancelled ! the reserve-fund and the un-
divided profits strike one as being the falsest
creations that any distinguished Victorian poli-
tician ever crowded into a balance sheet.
—, —
Owing to the encroachments of landlordism in
the United Kingdom, Great Britain was unable
to grow enough wheat to support her domestic
population in 1840, and hence the necessity of
the destruction of deer-parks or recourse to
foreign agriculturists to feed the nation.
Under Socialistic conditions the position would
stand thus : Total acreage of United Kingdom,
77 millions of acres. Assuming 50 per cent,
(this is liberal enough) to consist of unsuitable
soil, and 25 per cent, to be requisite for pur-
poses other than wheat cultivation, this would
leave 19£ millions of acres available for self-
support. Averaging 30 bushels to the acre, and
51 bushels to the yearly consumption per head
of population, Great Britain would be able to
feed 90,250,000 persons. What are the facts,
however, to - day ? She has under crop
only 2,463,000 acres, producing millions of
bushels, or just enough to feed 15 millions of
persons, or not much more than a third of her
people ! The question now to be faced in the
old country, then, amounts to this : Either
nationalisation must be introduced, to he followed
hy a strong protective tariff, or else land mono-
poly must be permitted to flourish accompanied
hy Freetrade as far as food is concerned, seeing
that under present circumstances Protection
would be useless in a country which could not
possibly supply its local market requirements.
Freetrade and monopoly are synonymous terms.

Extract from a recent report by Sir William
M‘Gregor, the pious boss of British New
Guinea: —
Mr Fellowes lately visited some of the Misinia villages.
When he had, with the help of the chief, collected the
inhabitants of a certain village to divine service, he asked
the people to kneel down for prayer. They, of course,
laughed at him and refused. For some tune both sides
held out, Mr. Fellowes refusing to proceed with the
service and they declining to kneel. But in the end they
knelt down and closed their eyes, and Mr. f ellowes went
on with the service. Under such guidance the progress
of the mission cannot but be rapid and fruitful.
And yet when the native had knelt down and
closed his eyes while a white man, whom he
probably took for a dangerous lunatic, jabbered
something in an unknown tongue, he got just
as much spiritual benefit as if he had stood on
his head while somebody read over the multi-
plication table. The man who carries the
Gospel to the pore heathen and the man who
carries Euclid to a cow generally make about
the same impression upon the audience.
A Hot Beginning.
When the old Sydney Theatre Royal (destroyed
by fire on Friday morning last) opened on Satur-
day, December 11, 1875, poor Sam Lazar, the
lessee, was hugely delighted to be able to secure
the attendance of the stout Sir Hercules Robinson,
his large lady, the St. Johns and Hutchinsons,
and the rest of them, for the creed of Sydney
society, 17 years ago, certainly was—of such is the
Kingdom of Heaven. So Lazar prepared a
spectacle for the edification of their mightinesses
which was to keep them his special friends for
ever and to make his fortune into the bargain. It
was not the piece. Oh, dear no ! That was a
tame affair called Daisy Farm” wherein G. 13.
Russell murdered J. P. West, who offended every-
body terribly, not so much by coming to life again
himself, but because he thereby prevented Russell
from being hanged for putting an end to him.
The climax of the ceremony was to have been
reached at the end of the second act, when
addresses and speeches, all carefully prepared and
highly laudatory of Sir Hercules Robinson, Mr.
Lazar, and other notable personages, were to be
delivered from the stage. But the night was a
broiler, the hottest of all that hot season, and
Lady Robinson was fat and scant of breath. She
came, she saw, she sweltered. Her temper was as
large as her skirts, and she wanted to go at the
end of the first act. There was not a breath
of air in the theatre. Lazar had carefully
announced who made the seats, who
covered them, and who gilded them ; who
decorated the ceiling and who' carpeted the Hour ;
who made the lamps and who supplied the fixtures.
But he had forgotten to state who arranged the
ventilation- in fact, he had forgotten to provide
any. My lady’s purpose was foiled, for when she
tried to rise, Sir Hercules fairly held her down,
while the gallant St. John and the athletic
Hutchinson went and broke the windows to give
her some little ease. It was a vain expedient,
for the windows opened on nowhere. As the
curtain descended on the second act, fhe. pof
spiring lady flounced from her seat and tied, nir
Hercules obediently following, and Society, which
‘could not stay after dear Lady Robinson had
gone,” rushing precipitately after. The rest o
the audience departed in search of beer, and no
man alive knows whether these addresses were
duly delivered. Indeed, the piece itself.would
have been plaved out in solitude, but for t e at
that Sam Lazar had hospitably bidden a lot of
men to a supper at Polly Smith’s tavern, next
door, to follow the performance, and they were,
in common decency, bound to show up after the
interval.
# * *
According’ to the description afforded to the
public by the prospectuses, “the dress circle was
a luxurious lounge,” and “the theatre replete
with every convenience, accommodation and com-
fort that architectural skill could devise.’’ We
had scented almanacs and Christmas cards pre-
sented to us as we went in, and we had
the pleasure of seeing a number of venerable
and respectable personages, many of whom still
adorn the stage, doing their best to be gay and
frisky in a temperature of 14G deg. and an atmos-
phere of Tophet. Those who were lucky enough
to sit on the o.p. side might see Mr. Lazar in the
prompt entrance jumping and stamping about in
a vain endeavour to get the artists to liven the
thing un, as if anything in the world could
liven “Daisy Farm.” It was a poor sort of piece
based upon an incident which had been done to
death five hundred times before. A vagrant who
knows that a wife has been previously married to a
man something likehimself in face and figure,comes
back and pretends to be the deceased hubby. The
lady goes into varied hysterics, the second hus-
band gives the tramp =£soo to begone, the lady s
scapegrace son, who would be the returned hero s
first-born if the returned iiero did not turn out to
be somebody else, throws his supposed parent over
a precipice to get the .£5OO, and everything looks
very blue for everybody when a wandering tourist
named Craven recognises the tramp as a dis-
charged soldier who had been drummed out of his
own regiment, and calls out in a tone of command
“ Jiichard Clarke ! ” whereat the impostor salutes,
and so shows that he cannot be the defunct
Burridge. All me, how dull it was, to be sure,
and how glad we were when it was all over to
console ourselves with the cold collation and
lukewarm drinks of Polly Smith, and to pretend
that the whole business had been a big success !
» # *
On the stage that night were Adelaide Bowring,
Kate Douglas, Maggie Oliver (the only one of the
party who was endurably vivacious), and Dolly
Forde. Also, W. J. Holloway, pretending to be
a cool and careless tourist —“I never carry lug-
gage, and you may track me all over Europe by
cast-off paper-collars ” —E. B. Russell, playing at
being a juvenile actor ; Joyce, Daniels, Boston, C.
P. Carey, and other long-standing institutions of
this great and glorious country. Tito reserve
forces included Kate Arden, Nelly Greenless, Mrs.
Wooldridge, Miss Melville, J. D. Marsh, Hasher,
and others; the one spark of youth and genius in
the lot being our special and particular Myra
Kemble, “than whom,” &c. Apropos, she is just
back from Maoriland, looking lovely.
* « *
There was a rare scene on Friday morning when
the fire was announced, and Adonis Stuart and
Hercules Standing and all the rest of the gallant
and gorgeous gentry of the company tore them-
selves from the arms of beauty or the slumbers of
innocence, as the case might be, and bolted off to
see what could be saved from “ the devouring
element.” For it is no joke to lose your props.
A sweetheart may be picked up at any time, but
the wigs of Clarkson and Toy, the garments of
May or Monsieur Alias, the grease-paints of
Germany, the rouge and pads of Paris, and the
multifarious odds and ends of a stage existence
cost time and money, and are not to be got every
day everywhere. Happily everything of that sort
was saved, and the public will still be able to
delight themselves by gazing on the same old com-
plexions and calves which have already enchanted
the gallery, enthralled the circle, and set all the
damsels in the stalls inditing love-letters by the
bushel to the shapely and stalwart heroes of the
footlights.

A more remarkable series of blunders than that
perpetrated in Saturday’s Sydney Morning-
Herald concerning the fire at the Theatre Royal
has rarely distinguished any newspaper. It gives
the date of opening in half-a-dozen places as
September 11, 1875, instead of December 11 m
that year; it speaks of the building as “being
the scene of so many histrionic triumphs from
Ristori to Bernhardt,” forgetting that Riston
always played at the other shop, the Victoria, and
left Australia finally one week before the Royal
was opened, sailing from Adelaide on December 4,
1875; and it also makes the Royal the scene of
“ the first production of ‘Struck Oil,’ by Maggie
Moore and J. C. Williamson,” who had long
before left for Europe, their final engagement
before their departure in Sydney being played at
the old Queen’s Theatre in July, 1875. Ihese and
many others are blunders which might have been
remedied by -five minutes’ time devoted to the
Herald’s file.

Toko Arakino, a Maori charged at Auck-
land with breaking up a school, pleaded that he
had “ no brains in his head and couldn’t work for
a living.” We know several with Toko’s trouble,
but they wear bigger collars and are not so candid.
Toko got 12 months, subject to medical exam.
From the Sporting Times
Mr. Fairie, whose horse Galeopsis ran so well in the
Two Thousand, became a millionaire in a remarkable
manner. Fora time he was in low water in Australia,
when he came across a man to whom he had lent i-100.
The man could not pay him, but said that he had an
original share in the Broken Hills which he would let him
have for £l5O. This was refused, and it was finally
decided to plav five games of bcart<s to decide whether
the price should be £2OO or £lOO. Mr. Fairie won, and
out of this hundred pounds he cleared one million sterling,
and it would have been three millions if he had stuck to
the whole of the share.
Who is “Mr. Fairie?”
From a twaddling review of a twaddling
novel, “The Shelling of the Peas,’’one can extract
a fair idea of the modern lady’s novel, and its
favourable press notice —“In the first instance,
‘one of the magnates of the neighbourhood,’Mr.
Denzil Seymour, catches her at her work. ‘ Shel-
ling peas,’ he exclaimed, ‘ how delightful. I have
not been permitted that delightful diversion since
I was in the nursery, when nurse used to bring me
some pods by way of a treat. Miss Millicent,
allow me to help yon ; peas always had a fascina-
tion for me.’ As may be imagined, Seymour is
the heroine’s fate” —remarks the idiot reviewer
who singles out this balderdash for admiration.
It doesn’t say much for Millicent that the reader
is expected to see this “fate” of hers at a glance.
What a fate I To be hopelessly mashed by a
Denzil-the everlasting Denzil of three-volume
novels—who talks of enjoyable diversions, and
vows that peas always had a fascination for him.
Anv reasonable heroine would kill Denzil in the
second chapter, and Stew him along with ti e
vegetables.
Good-Bye, Ireland! *
(Being the reply of an English Socialist to a “Patriot
request to “ once more co-operate in work for the good
old Came of Home Rule for Ireland.”)
Good-bye, Ireland ! I’ve loved you long: and well,
I loved you first in the days when you lay on your
face in hell,
With the whip on your fleshless shoulders and the
goad in your hollow flank.
And the blood-suckers, blind in blood clung on to
your limbs and drank ;
In the days when your servile priests sat chalk-
faced, chewing meal,
And abjured you to do no murder, and, much
more, not to steal.
But now it is very different. Their speech is high
and free,
Your bishops and priests surround you, a goodly
company!
You've got Walsh the Wobbler. What do you
want with me ?
Good-bye, Ireland ! In those days, in this moral
land of mine,
There were no godly and greasy souls to bluster,
rant and whine
At the age-long tale of your wrongs, at the spec-
tacle of your woes,
But we faced alone for your sake the winds, the
rains, the snows,
And the love wherewith we loved you and the
trust in you we had
Were a new hope in the human heart to make us
proud and glad.
But now it is very different. Your talk’s but of
“policy,”
And the old Liberal Gang’s around you, and
they give you three times three,
You’ve got Gladstone, the Gobbler, What do
you want with me ?
Good-bye, Ireland ! I ask you what did you do
With the Man who won life and freedom and
victory for you ?
Who alone of all mankind came down to you there
in hell?
Who lifted you up and kissed you, and beat back
your foemen fell ?
Who clothed you and arrayed you in the garb of a
nation great,
And put at last in your hands the shining sceptre
of Fate ?
He loved another man’s wife, you say, and there-
fore you and She,
The shop-keeping English Wolf, your friend, you
throttled him—righteously.
You’ve got Healy the Hell-Cat. What do you
want with me ?
Good-bye, Ireland ! We've woes and wrongs of
our own ;
In our reeking alleys they gibber ; in our rotten
cots they moan,
And you’ve shown us they’re nothing to you,
except to barter and sell,
Any more than that Man who raised you from out
of the gutter of hell.
And if Wobblers, and Gobblers, and Hell-Cats to
your heaven can bring you in.
We’re free to admit that you stand a very fair
chance to win.
But this is very different. You’re a clever jade
Play out your little game—play it very carefully.
Get into heaven, or go back to hell! It’s the
same to me !
FRANCIS ADAMS.
* “ I don’t know if you’re aware,” wrote a correspon-
dent to The Bulletin by a recent mail, “ that at present
the caucus of the Irish party is almost in the hands of
Tim Healy, who is backed up by Archbishop Walsh and
the bishops, and that it is now necessary for a man to be
a ‘ Cartholic ’ to have any chance at all with the crowd.
This state of things is infernal, and not the least infernal
part of it is the acquiescence of the old Liberal gang, who
run Gladstone, and of the young Nonconformist gang,
with Brice Hughes and Stead at their head. The Old
Man has just refused to receive a Labour deputation in re
the Eight Hours question, and the whole crowd (Morley
with them) are shutting down as hard as they can on any-
thing but the old stock Liberal tommy-rot.”
Old Swan, the millionaire contractor, was
profoundly impressed by seeing “ Money ” acted,
at Melbourne Bijou, with the scene of the
expectant legatees over the reading of the will;
and, says he, “ Same here,” being a widower with
a grown-up family all able to earn their own liveli-
hood. “Why don't he die ?—that’s what they’re
saying,” said he, whereupon he married the beau-
tiful Ruby Skinner. Old Swan became a swell,
knocking the money down with his pretty wife,
new mansion, britzka and pair, and goodness
knows what, and when he died he left all the
balance to the Victorian Government, to keep the
Melbourne Observatory going, in emulation of
the noble Californian Jim Lick.
In a Melbourne daily newspaper-office
were two gentlemen of the same name, but in no
way related. One is editor of a sporting weekly
published from the same office. The other is a
comp, on the daily. Say the name is Wolff. Mr.
Wolff, the sporting editor, was blissfully uncon-
scious of the existence of Wolff, the comp., until
one day a writ was served upon him in his sanctum
at the 'instance of a small suburban time-payment
barber. The staff remembers still that Mr. Wolff
swore horribly, and was unapproachable until,
after protracted enquiries, he discovered that a
namesake of his had been born and was then
working at “case ” upstairs. The writ was then
successfully passed on to its proper destination,
and the editorial profanity vanished into thin air.
A few months later, however, a grocery-bill of
respectable dimensions was found by Mr. Wolff
among his letters at the office, and as he
had never heard of the grocer, nor of the items,
his expressions of displeasure had more cayenne
in them than on the previous occasion. The bill
was promptly forwarded to Wolff, the comp. In
the fullness of time a second writ was served upon
Mr. Wolff, the editor, and this was followed by
variegated bills for a choice assortment of articles
ranging from socks to perambulators. Instead,
however, of bowing to the decrees of use and
wont, and enjoying the thing now that it seemed
inevitable, Mir, Wolff began to talk bloodthirsty,
and wild ideas of murder as a justifiable pastime
found expression even in his leisure hours. But
the climax had yet to come. Some weeks after the
disastrous floods of last year, Mr. Wolff, the
editor, on arriving one morning found on his table
a massive communication from the Town Hall,
intimating that his application for assistance from
t u Central Floods Relief Fund had been refused,
enquiries having elicited that he was earning a
substantial income as editor of the , and that
the committee could not, in view of that fact,
regard his case as one of bonci-iidc distress. For
some months after that eventful and stormy
morning, a wild eyed and fading editor mechani-
cally passed every suspicious-looking missive up to
the comps.’ room, and a few weeks ago the pro-
prietors, in order to restore him to health and
reason, generously consented to —well, to add
unfortunate Wolff, the comp., to the ranks of the
unemployed. Mr. Wolff is progressing favourably.

816 Macquari c-street North, Sydney, June 22,1892
My Dbar Moorabinda, —
From a sartorial point of view, “ La Cigale,” at Her
Majesty’s, is very taking, because there isn’t a pleated
petticoat anywhere in the opera. The management has
sliced the ballerinas’ frocks into hanging panels (you can’t
kick in gores, you know) rather than bore the audience
with another surfeit of the concertina skirt. Marie
Halton, as Marton, didn’t dress to flabbergasting lengths.
Marie (viewed with feminine dispassion) is the usual
plump prima-donna with a comic-opera nose “ shaped to
catch the rain,” as they say in Paris, and a clever way of
dressing herself, except in the middle act, perhaps. When
a star is what the “ found-drowned ” reports call “ well-
nourished,” let her beware of glove-fits in satin as she
would of a wrinkle in her silken hose.
In the first act Marton wears a pink and floral costume
with a big hat and her hair down -an artless-village-
maidish get-up which becomes her better than either of
her other glories. There is probably consummate art on
her side. Does not the girl from Dough Flat look much
more charming in farm rags and her brother’s old
bluchers than when she’s got up in her Sunday cashmere
and parasol? In act two, Marton, in trained yellow
satin and a diamond necklace and black lace petticoats
beneath, might have been the mamma of the skittish
creature of the opening scene. The last gorgeous act
(en parenihise, a rainbow is the only thing that might
find something to sneer at in the staging of “ La Cigale
a mere drab-coloured individual can only sit in dazzled
astonishment while the stage-manager and the limelight
man are making his or her eyes water with a three-act
sketch of clamorous colour) revealed La Cigale looking
slimmer in cream brocade flowered with crimson and
green ; underdress of cream filmy material and golden
girdle-an easy fitting bit of apparelling which the light-
ning change to black rags and guitar and back again
made necessary, I suppose.
The corps du theatre had every stitch of canvas on, so
to speak, from the smallest baby jigging in the miniature
ballet to—well—whoever considers him or herself the
starting-point of the troupe. Flora Graupner’s white
brocade and silver wedding-dress was a dainty turn-out,
and Florence Young’s last array of pale-green brocade
with pink flowers mingled with pink silk and tan-
coloured lace took a trick, but I didn’t care for the
bizarre dressing of the ptihnibre danseuse. She fluttered
in as many gay odds-and-ends as a “sacrifice remnant”
counter. Nevertheless, she and the prima-donna took all
the floral tributes flying round at the opening. Yum-yum!
Discussing the dressing of “La Cigale,” it is pertinent
to wonder why they didn’t have the French novelty-
garters for the danseuses? It’s the latest idea. Bal-
lerinas wear their elongated hosen ostensibly tied above
the knee with long ribbon garters of contrasting hues,
which give a distracting effect to the high kick and must
add greatly to the delight of the bald-headed man in the
front stalls. I read of one effective creature lashing out
in snow-white, cap-k-pie, but with a black ribbon garter
fastened with diamonds.
Meditating over effects of face and figure in “ London
Assurance " I found another (rather delicate) objection to
the abandonment of corsets. As every woman must
know, the tighter the stay the higher and more rounded
becomes the bust, and when no stays are worn the
frontal outline is miserably flat and floppy-looking. The
only advantage—and I suppose in this bare-backed age
it is an advantage—l can see is, that the uncorseted
woman may wear her gowms generously scooped out
frontally in a more abysmal V than her busked sister can,
but I question if a wide-open frock flutterin ground a limp
fall-abroad outline is half so “ chipper ” as a bodice with,
say, half the shutters up, but neatly fitted over a visible
rise and fall ? In some respects a Kanaka belle and a
seraph with no stays on have very much in common.
Gazing at Mrs. Deere as Lady Gay Spanker, it was
cruelly evident that that uncorseted divinity ought to go
hunting in a tea-gown if she wants to look “her own
woman,” for anything more slummocky or uncomfortable-
looking than Mrs. B.’s brown habit on her first appearance
in “London Assurance,” it has never been my luck to
see. Afterwards, Richard was herself again in a
puzzling dinner-dress, rather voluminous skirtally, of
melon-pink erfipe, mostly draped with black gauze, de-
signed in gold, bodice folded in pink and black, and many
dazzling diamonds.
Miss Urquhart’s Paris figure was draped in a pale-grey
frock, very well moulded round the hips; and a cream
guipure over violet silk, with big sleeves, and trimmed
with lines of purple velvet, which formed a sash for the
waist. By the way, quite a rustle ran thro’ the gods, and
I heard one faint, far-away “Or-der-er!” when Lady Gay
talked of her horse Blueskin to Teddy Royce, who stood
by in the character of Sir Harcourt, got-up to perfection
in a dyed wig and rouged cheeks. But has Herbert
Standing gone to a ring-dove for ideas on his ocular
make-up?
One thing nobody seems to have noticed about Mr.
Walter Bentley is his ladylike finger-tips, where the filbert
nails shine like mother-o’-pearl. Mr. Bentley is a firm
believer in manicure, and his digit-points are quite
encouraging to beginners in the nail-improving art.
Apropos of personal appearance, I want to give a word
of comfort to the girl with the freckled face. If the spots
are acquired and not bequeathed by some distant ancestor
who may have resembled a frost-bitten orange as he hung
by his tail from a tree, then they only speckle your speakr
Ing frontispiece just as long as you want them to. For
those annoying specks which pepper one’s nose so after a
ride, or a picnic, or any such gambol in the sun, a certain
cure has been discovered. Here is the recipe:—Two
drachma of sal-ammonia” with an ounce of German
cologne. Mix with a pint of distilled water and apply two
or three times a day.
Talking about freckles somehow reminds me that the
very latest idea is photographing people in their natural
colours. But is it an unmixed blessing? Certainly the
present “photo.” is either a hdeoas caricature or a
nauseating flattery, but we are used to it, and when we
come out profiled to Grecian point, when we are really
snubby, we almost love it. The world isn’t honest enough
for natural-coloured photographs. Let’s take the opinion
of the red-headed houri, the green-eyed girl, the
parchment-cheeked darlings, and, above all, the freckled
fairies.
The povv-wow at Sydney School of Arts strikes me as
being an eloquent argument against cock-and-hen
societies, and the shrill shriek of the lady-member (whose
subscription has been raised and whose reading-room for
“ many, many years,” as one fairy pathetically but un-
guardedly put it, has been taken away), and the pacifying
growl of the male member (who remains in statu, quo)
make it very clear that the lion and the lamb can’t share
the same stockyard yet awhile.
Mr. and Madame Kellermann gave a musical “at home”
last week at their residence, “Canala,” Potts Point, to a
crowd of people, who passed a charming time, of course :
for Madame Kellermann, in a Frenchy-looking dress of
sea-green silk and silver striped gauze, with green moire
sash and Watteau bow, played a solo in her brilliant,
fretful style ; Deslouis warbled like a lark ; and Poussard
fiddled until his diamond ring shot sparks.
Some pretty dresses found their way in, too. Mrs.
(Mayoress) Manning’s turn-out of golden brocade, sub-
dued with black velvet, was very clever ; her two
daughters, in pale-pink chiffon and black velvet, respec-
tively, w ere well contrasted ; Miss Florrie Hill sported
pale-cream and green brocade; Miss Nellie Hill’s frock
seemed to consist of folds of sea-green aerophane ; Miss
Ethel Manning couldn’t have chosen a more becoming
get-up than her pale-blue tulle and diamonds; Mrs, Garvam
Mrs. Williamson and Mrs. (Dr.) Laure wore black. Mrs
8. Hordern’s was a very intricate garment ; pale-grey and
pale-strawberry'-pink brocade formed the bodice and train,
the jupe of pink silk, covei’ed with black jetted tulle, the
Medici collar lined with pink ostrich feathers.
Apropos music. Miss Bessie Doyle has returned to
Sydney, from Leipsic, is a rather pretty girl of
twenty' years or so, with a big book of German critic,
isms of the most marmalade-ish description and a wonder-
ful violin. She makes her professional re-entrde at
soon, in a short series of concerts under the management
of Mr. Charles Bowler, and Sydney is sure to give its own
particular prodigy a warm welcome. Among the German
criticisms aforesaid, Miss Bessie is alluded to as the third
violinist lady in the world, and judging by these Teutonic
remarks (for the lady refuses to play a note for friend or
foe in private), Australia has eveiy' reason to be proud of
Bessie.
Sydney Women’s College is always chivvying some wild
idea or other, and hardly a day passes without a well-
dressed deputation bearding somebody to see if they can
have something. The latest troop wanted to have the
site set apart in the 'Varsity grounds for the Public
Instruction Training Schools given over to the Women’s
College building, in support of which demand it w'as
pointed out that the first-named site was nearerthe public
road, and was in many respects more suited for the pur-
pose of a Women’s College. Either site would be equally
good for the students of the Training College.
But this is rank injustice. As if the girls in training
for public-school billets don’t want to look innocently out
of the window as well as the Women’s College students!
But, after all, the training schools are not wholly given
over to the girls, as the Ladies’ College is, and I really
think, with the deputation, that it’s right they should
have the best position for catching the public gaze.
I hear from Goulburn that Mrs. Dalglish (now in
England), one of the rich Broken Hill widows, is going to
marry a Mr. Bellasis, a middle-aged gentleman with a
large family and little money'. He belongs, though, to
“ a good old Catholic family,” and this may make amends
for the disparity in fortune. The happy couple are
going to reside at Lulworth Castle, the home of the
Weld-Blundells in Lancashire.
The accidental death of Governor Jersey’s unfortunate
aide-de-camp, the Earl of Ancram, has dashed the matri-
monial hopes of (among a host of others) a very charming
Southern society girl. But she should dry her pretty
eyes, for papa (the Marquis) rvould never have heard of
the match. Flirting with an earl (unless you are titled
too) is usually a game without a goal, unless he happens
to wear a very inferior sort of ancestral holt, and has got
down to be a billiard-marker.
A very pretty costume ball was given by Mr. and Mrs.
Bransby at their residence, “ Cambridge Villa,” Coogee,
on 17th inst., in honour of Mrs. Bransby’s eldest daughter,
Miss Stewart, attaining her majority. The four daughters
of the house represented the seasons—Miss Stewart, as
“Autumn,” wearing an artistic brown dress, With red
poppies and wheat-ears. Mrs. Bransby', who made a tire-
less hostess, wore a black satin brocade gown. Speeches
were made over the big birthday cake, and a special tram
took everybody back to town at 2 a.m.

lithographed on the front-page of the “ Hug me Again ”
valse.

Bijou.,, “The Country Girl.”
“ La Cigale,” with the fair Marie Halton as
theheroine, made its dehut at Her Majesty ’s(Sydney)
in the-presence of a crowded and enthusiastic
audience, which afterwards cooled off visibly. This
result was not surprising, for “La Cigale ” is a
woefully unsatisfactory opera. It is always on
the verge of doing something, yet never does it.
The heroine is a frivolous maiden with not much
brain and no thought for the future, and at the
beginning the author seems to imply that some-
thing is going to happen to her, but nothing does
happen. She has a faithful but despairing lover
who has no earthly business in the bill unless his
fidelity is going to be rewarded, for he has nothing
to do save to hang round and wait for better times ;
yet the curtain falls and leaves him hanging. The
wicked though gaudy Duke “lays for” La Cigale in
the first act, apparently with a sinful purpose; but
the purpose doesn’t come off, and the wicked Duke
isn’t confounded —in fact, nothing happens to him
at all. He doesn’t even say anything of impor-
tance, apparently because he hasn’t anything to
say. His wife leads a certain Chevalier astray,
and the Duke comes upon them while they are
sinfully mashing each other in a summer-house,
and then he goes away without seeing anything,
lie never does see anything, in fact. When the
Chevalier casts the lady off, and falls honestly in
love with another girl, she vows a fearful ven-
geance, and doesn’t even attempt to carry it
out instead, she comes back smiling, and
becomes the sincere friend of everybody. La
Cigale has a portentous vision, but as it
doesn’t come true, and nothing more is heard
about it, she might as well have a cold
bath. A very, very sad and ancient mendicant
intrudes himself into the opera, but he is nobody’s
long-lost father, and nobody recognises him, and
he hasn’t a message for anybody—he simply stays
awhile and then drifts away. The good girl of
the story marries a bumpkin with a head like an
evergreen tree, and the girl who isn’t so good
married a rich nobleman —apparently as a solemn
warning to all girls not to be too good. Both
these girls are the daughters of a sausage-maker
who was hanged. With only about two excep-
tions the songs have no connection with the
alleged plot ;“they are mostly harmless little things
about summer and zephyrs, and bovvers and
breezes, and they are led up to with difficulty by
somebody who makes a remark about the weather.
Sometimes they are not led up to at all, but are
simply dragged in by the ear.
# * *
These are a few of the disadvantages
“La Cigale.” Among its advantages are Charles
Ryley, whose Chevalier is an almost faultless
performance ; and the evergreen Howard Vernon,
who does all that man can do for the dancing,
leering Duke —that nobleman who is always on the
verge of being found out by Providence or by his
wife, yet trots along serenely and dodges all the
thunderbolts that ought to hit him and lay him
low. Also, the long-lost J. C. Williamson
reappears as the uncle of the sausage-maker’s
daughters, and makes a beauteous Dutchman who
is not John Stofel, but another Dutchman, pro-
bably his long-lost brother. Pretty little Flora
Graupner is as sweet and successful as a
thankless part will permit, and Miss Florence
Young, as the Duchess, whose oui’se doesn’t come
off and whose anathema misses fire, is a revelation,
inasmuch as she makes the dead waste of that
arid female blossom like the rose, and infuses
life into what was obviously meant for an automa-
ton. Last of all, Miss Marie Halton —we have
left her to the last in the hope ttiat these remarks
may not meet her eye—is a disappointment, and
only fills us with longings to have Billee Barlow
back again regardless of cost. She is a round
lady in a square character, and we say, with sad-
ness, that the lady is very round, and the character
is very square. Also, the scenery is magnificent,
the orchestra is commendable, the ballet is a thing
of unspeakable joy, and the dialogue deserves to
be hanged by the neck till it is dead —dead—and
utterly deceased.
m# * *
Last Saturday night the partially-com-
busted company from Sydney Royal rose from
its ashes, the Criterion being the scene of the
resurrection. The advertised programme for the
week consisted of three nights of “School for
Scandal,” two more of “ She Stoops to Conquer,”
and a brief resurrection of “Adrienne Lecouvreur.”
The devouring element has left no ashes on Mrs.
Beere’s azure brow, and her Lady Teazle is a
faultless picture of how a woman, who is meant
for better things, may be found behind a screen,
and experience great difficulty in explaining
matters afterwards. It is a sufficient proof of Mrs.
Beere’s genius, that she makes the explanation
appear so absolutely credible, that every separate
male individual in the audience felt prepared to
accept it without hesitation. The statement, as
far as mere words go, is feeble and inconclusive,
and only one actress in a thousand, being found
hidden in a strange snufflebuster’s apartment, can
offer it in such a way as to get through without
any injury to her character. Mr. Herbert Stand-
ing would have been a first-class Charles Surface,
only nature has made him a trifle too massive for
the billet, and it has also made Otlio Stuart
scarcely oily enough for the part of Joseph. He
leaves an impression behind him that he ought to
go and lead some cheaper person astray, and
someone whose fall wouldn’t be so much regretted.
When an Eve of so many attractions and so much
brain is to be tempted, a serpent of the biggest
and shiniest order should be engaged for the job,
and Otlio Stuart, though a large and polished
dramatic reptile, is hardly large enough. M.
Marius had a small show as Trip, and got level by
elevating that mannikin to unexpected import-
ance, and E. W. Royce came out in all his wonted
brilliance as Uncle Surface—the first of that long
line of dramatic uncles who keep turning up on
the stage even unto this day. Also, Miss Isabella
Urquhart was Lady Sneerwoll, and was eminently
successful as such ; the dresses were gorgeous and
showed no signs of having been dragged out from
beneath the ruins of the fallen Royal; the fittings,
fixtures and utensils were all that could be
desired ; and Mr, Cathcart, as Sir Peter, was
blessed with the privilege of being for two brief
hours the fleeting husband of the star. He seemed
to deserve the billet,‘■htnl when he found the lady
under the painful mrciimstances aforementioned
he accepted her explanations in a manner which
did him credit.
The Jubilee Singers and Virginia Concert
Company are still gathering large audiences at the
Sydney Centenary Hall. The three things which
are certain to command unlimited public support
in this blasb generation are a glaring leg-show, a
particularly sinful divorce-case—and a company of
singers who warble sweetly the pleasant, innocent
songs of a time when the world was young and
comparatively ignorant of tights. Why this
strange combination should be no one knows;
possibly even legs pall after a time. The Jubilee
Singers have no tights to recommend them, and
there isn t even one authentic divorce-case among
the crowd. But they are among the most
melodious songsters who have ever invaded
New South Wales, and when they are comic
they fill the house with mirth, and when
they are pathetic a silent tear rolls down the
rouged countenance of the dress-circle and is
deposited as an unassuming tribute on the, bald
head of the stalls. A good part of the time they
are pathetic, and on the whole we prefer them
that way. There is a great fund of pathos in
many of their melodies, and in this world decent
pathos is even more scarce than decent comedy—
what is called pathos is mostly maunderings.
There are hardly any weak points in the company,
but Mr. Orpheus M‘Adoo, “the deepest basso
in the world,” and Miss Mattie Allen are about
the strongest. If the former is not actually the
deepest basso in the world, he is at least deep
enough to satisfy all possible requirements ; and
the latter is a gem in the realm of melody and a
tenor of marvellous melody. The Jubilee Singers
—why they are Jubilee isn’t stated—will go on at
the present address till further notice.

At a late hour on Saturday night, some o£
the first families of New South Wales went forth
secretly in a sinister-looking punt, painted black,
and, in the absence of the watchman, scuttled the
convict hulk Success, and sent her down suddenly
to the bottom of the harbour. The ominous old
relic is now in the depths of Kerosene Bay, and
all the wax convicts went down with her to leer
among the fishes, and glare with evil countenances
at the crabs. There is no published evidence
about the cause of the disaster, but The
Bulletin advances the above theory with con-
fidence. It was exceedingly rough on the
aristocracy that its grandfather should be ex-
hibited to the public as a dead monstrosity
in a cage, and it became necessary to take des-
perate measures. Therefore the uppah classes
went out disguised in the darkness, and pulling
Avith muffled oars across the waters they solemnly
dug a hole in the hated object, and with a sudden
bubbling groan it canted over and went down.
The watchman returned just as it was sinking,
but he only waited on board for a single minute,
and then he fled for Ins life. He ascribes the
trouble to the ravages of the cobra in the plank-
ing, but he is Avrong —it was the ravages of the
Legislative Council armed Avith a bis auger that
did it.

“ The Bells,” that weird spectacle of mys-
tery, visions, and madness, is the bill-of-fare this
week at Sydney Garrick. “ The Bells ”is strictly
a one-man play, and Walter Bentley, in a robust
sort of way, is that man. The plot is simple enough
—in his early life Mathias, the publican, murdered
a Hebrew among the snowdrifts, and took his
money, and cremated his body in a lime-kiln ; and
fifteen years afterwards the spectral sledge of
the deceased takes to coming along the road
on snowy nights, and the clatter of the bells
tells Mathias when it is, there. He grows
haggard and wretched, and is haunted by
dreams, and imagines that a fiend is ringing the
dinner-bell on the stairs in Hades, and at last he
has a worse dream than usual and goes mad, and
perishes barking and howling on the stage, Avhile
the orchestra, with an awful sense ot the unfitness
of things, keeps time to his delirium with the
joyous melody of the “Dutchman’s leetle daAvg.”
It is a splendidly tragic scene —that stricken man
dying in the arms of his terrified daughter and her
husband, his old white head like the head of
Terror incarnate, his shrill voice rending the air
in frantic shrieks, while the orchestra cheerfully
inquires in music, “ O vere and O vere ish mine
leetle dawg gone ?” But apart from this dis-
crepancy, Mr. Bentley is a success. His burgo-
master is a notable villain, Avith such a crafty
expression in the thousands of croAvsfeet about the
corners of his cold, scheming eye, and in the
furrows on his parchment brow. His leathern
visage is Aveather-beaten and pious looking like
one of those holy, hard-praying marauders, the
Beggars of the Sea, and the only objectionable
feature in his character is that he looks too crafty,
and iS too obviously a man with a burdened soul,
and that he shouts too fiercely at times when the
bells begin to clatter outside his door, and that he
has a strong Scotch accent and says he will put
the key in “me pawket.” Miss Laura Hansen
looks pretty and dresses nicely as the burgo-
master’s daughter. Her chief business is to fall
into Christian’s arms at intervals, and she does
it to perfection. Mr. 11. R. Roberts is Christian,
and is a beautiful young man. Mr. L. McGowan
and Mr. W. F. Clitherow play two village
tipplers, and barring the fact that the former is
distinctly Scotch at times, they arc both good.
Then we get a back-view of our dear old friend
Bill South, seen through a green glass darkly in the
burgomaster’s dream. Also, Miss Grace 1 lamilton
plays a slipshod, gushing slavey, with bare arms,
and a feeble intellect, and an irrepressible giggle,
and a tendency to read tattered, greasy, dog-
eared penny dreadfuls late at night by the
kitchen fire, and to hold candles at an acute
angle so that they gutter dreadfully on the floor ;
and apart from the sinful burgomaster himself she
is the only person in the drama who really attracts
us. The Bulletin is of opinion that her salary
ought to be trebled right off, and paid quarterly
in advance.

Mr. Larmour, in addition to booming the
Coogee Aquarium, has now opened the Sydney
Royal Standard on Saturdays and Sundays. On
Saturday afternoons a “children’s festival” is
held, which includes conjuring, music, acrobatic
Eerformanc s by the Arabs that Sells’ Circus left
ehind it, and Punch and Judy. Saturday evening
is devoted to farce and burlesque ; and the Sunday
evening alleged concerts are a humorous but
unholy device embracing singing, legerdemain,
and other show's which are calculated to bring
down fire and brimstone on a godless city.
** * *
There is little probability of Sydney
Theatre Royal being re-built. In the first place,
the ground lease has only four years to run, and,
secondly, it is improbable that the Government
Avould grant a license should the building be re-
instated. The ground landlords are understood
to be religious people and a considerable time ago
expressed their determination to demolish the
theatre directly the lease fell in. Now that the
building instead of the lease has fallen in, the
ground landlords will probably utilise the site for
another and more godly purpose.

The Sydney Royal management had con-
siderable trouble in arranging terms with Mr.
George Hill for a lease of the Criterion to finish
the Bernard-Beere season. This may be accounted
for by the fact that J. C. Williamson has been
identified with the new Lyceum, which is being
erected on the property of another branch of the
Hill family. To put matters mildly, Mr. George
Hill does not exactly approve of a new and better
house going up near his own.

“Joan of Arc” was substituted for
“ Faust Up to Date ” at Melbourne Opera House
on Saturday, without making muca apparent
difference. The people who strove to amuse in
the other burlesque repeated their attempts in
this. They accepted every encore they could get,
and when they couldn’t get it they took it just the
same. They came on singly, or in pairs, or in lots
at a time to do certain things, and they did as much
of each thing as a magnanimous public would per-
mit. On the whole, they possibly achieved a
brilliant success—any way, the performance passed
off without bloodshed, unless one of the many
chappies present happened to cut his throat for
very joy. Entertainments brighter and more
humorous than this have produced riots in
theatres ere now, whilst others even more distress-
ing have run for a long time. The Bulletin gives
“Joan of Arc ’’three weeks, although some judges
might like to give it the same number of years.
« # *
Lomieu is constantly appearing through-
out “Joan of Arc,” and he works hard on the
lines of a favourite comique who has been engaged
to do about fifteen “turns.” He comes along
once with vegetables in a barrow as Fred Leslie
did of yore, and sings another coster song. That
is to say, he takes a half-share in a coster duet,
for this burlesque introduces a brace of costers at
one fell swoop which is almost too much novelty.
Most of the plot, in fact, consists of Lonnen
bursting upon the scene in a different suit of
clothes to sing another song. Also, he dances
frequently and nimbly, and is more or less divert-
ing according to taste. Courtneidge has a satis-
factory say in the matter, and Marion Hood, in a
faint, intermittent travesty of Joan, contributes
two or three sentimental ballads. Alice Leamar
knock’s ’em again, especially with a music-hall
ditty about a girl who adopted the stage as a pro-
fession with an eye to hooking a viscount or a lord.
The one subtle joke of the show is embodied in
this item. Alice Lethbridge dances and speaks
her lines prettily, and Addie Conyers is likewise
pleasing in more than one respect. The others
seem to be overshadowed by the completeness of
the incidental arrangements. On Saturday even-
ing a floral tribute suitable for an elephant was
hoisted up from the orchestra with difficulty and
bestowed upon Alice Learner. Alice staggered
under her ghastly incubus in the first instance,
but eventually dragged it off in triumph. This
small token of esteem must have weighed about
two stone more than the Johnnie who gave the
order, and io looked an appropriate article for
dropping on his early grave by means of a crane.
* * *
At Melbourne Princess’, three indifferent
performances of “ Maritana ” were mercifully
concluded on Monday last. In contrast with the
Italian treatment of “Rigoletto” and “Lucia.”
these renderings of English opera seemed like so
many acts of cruelty, supported by a full orchestra.
The most cold-blooded offenders was His Majesty
of Spain, a gloomy, compact personage, who talked
and sang flat, and trampled upon the glorious
traditions of a fashionable theatre in an uncouth
manner. The Don Caesar of Henry Stookwell
was another depressing scion of an ancient race,
and although he might have fallen like a soldier
if they had given him the chance, he systematic-
ally failed to stand, walk, talk, or look like a
warrior, drunk or sober. Miss Baber did very
well in the name-part, Miss Ida Osborne gave
“ Alas ! those chimes ” in beautiful style, and
Alan Morris was a quietly effective Don Jose, but
this trio of satisfactory circumstances didn’t suffice
to leaven the whole lump. This week the Italian
Co. appear in “ La Sonnambula ” on Tuesday and
Friday, whilst the Foli combination concerts are
confidently expected to make the public roll up
and sit up on Thursday and Saturday.
*» * *
“The Bells of Haslemere,’’ first produced
in Melbourne some three years ago, was revived
at the Royal on Saturday, A house crowded
everywhere save in the dress-circle expressed its
satisfaction so vigorously that the drama in
question should last for more than a week, any-
how. Played with a new cast and considerable
alterations in the scenic arrangements at the end
of the third act, “ The Bells of Haslemere ” is a
stronger show than before, and it was always a
gorgeously illustrated story of consecutive interest.
The four male principals present reasonable cause
for transferring themselves to America, whilst the
characters they meet on the cotton-plantation,
having no ostensible reasons for popping up in
England an act later, don t pop up, but stay in
their own country and leave their fates to the
imagination. Henry Pettit would have preferred
to bring them across the Atlantic to assist in up-
setting the villain, no doubt, but Sydney Grundy
his oollaborateur, had literary scruples in the
matter and hung on to the eternal fitness of things
for the sake of his reputation. Thus it happens
that Miss Harrie Ireland is only a passing inci-
dent in the play, although her earnest, fresh,
young presentation of the cotton planter’s daugh-
ter gives a touch of pathos to the subsequent love
passages between a girl she never sees and a man
who has forgotten her. Others of the less ex-
perienced members of this company seem to have
a future before them and to be training on in the
way they should go. The leading people are all
well displayed in the present instance, and their
artistic success in the aggregate is perhaps a little
more marked than usual. Bland Holt scores
heavily at the blacksmith’s anvil. Walter Baker
frivols rather overmuch as the festive scamp, but
his dying exclamation of “deah boy” brings a
lump into every sympathetic throat, even though
it make the gallery Goths guffaw. Kate Bishop,
Mrs. Bland Holt, Edith Blande, Albert Norman,
and Charles Glenny are ever equal to the occasion,
but there are times when the last-named might
well quicken his tempo about half a bar to the
second.
# -* * *
Alfred Dampier’s friends and admirers
have decided to turn the ill-luck which has
troubled him of late by presenting that old
favourite with a testimonial on the occasion of a
benefit matinee to be given in his honour next
Saturday, at Melbourne Princess’s. The bill pre-
pared for this interesting occasion is strong
enough to fill the theatre twice over; so those who
find themselves unable to secure places for the
performance are advised to book seats in the testi-
monial, as it were, and send their little cheques
along to Richard Stewart, hon. treasurer,
Princess’s Theatre. Friends at a distance will
please accept this intimation. Mr. Dampier has
not been addicted to benefits, by any means. He
recently closed his long struggle at the Alexandra
without asking the public to rally round him or
any tiling of that sort, and the fact that he deserves
a bumper testimonial can’t be too generally
recognised.

! 5K- w
Among Mrs. Bernard Beere’s belongings
is a fine old housekeeper, who is never so happy
as when she is allowed to attend on her mistress
behind the scenes. One night Fedora had a cold,
and, as the theatre was very draughty, Mrs.
Cossit was given permission to wait at the wings
with a wrapper to put around the artist as the
curtain falls on the first act, the cue being Fedora’s
despairing cry, “ Vladimir! Dead ! ” and her
rush through the folding doors into the bedroom.
“You will know when to come, Mrs. Cossit?”
quoth Fedora. “Oh, yes, mum,” smilingly, “I
know exactly. It’s when you gives a rush, and
shrieks out ‘ Blanky near dead.’” And now
Fedora wonders how many of her audience hear
their favourite adjective in her exclamation.

The proprietorial history of Sydney
Theatre Royal is as follows : —Fitzgerald’s trustees
granted a 2i years’ building lease at a peppercorn
rent to Levy and Barnett, who built the Royal.
Levy and Barnett mortgaged to the Bank of New
South Wales, and eventually parted with the pro-
perty. Wilshire bought from the Bank, and sub-
let to Lazar at .£56 per week. Lazar sub let again
to Williamson, Garner, and Musgrove at <£9o per
week. Wilshire died, and his widow married
“ Ranny ” Want; consequently Mr. Want is
owner of the ground-lease, while Mrs. Moss
(Lazar’s sister) is simply tenant of the building.
