Tub Bulletin’s London correspondent The Fringe
of Society ” proved a most milk and watery affair, and
might have been hissed off the first night but tor powerful
acting at a critical point by Wyndham himself. The
Langtry wore some wonderful frocks, but even her
resplendent attire failed to make her Mrs. Eve Allen a
success—though she made the best of her part. The
author had made her altogether too respectable, and this
disappointed the audience. Mrs. Eve Allen is a bad 'un,
no doubt, but there isn’t any spice about her like the
Suzanne of Dumas. Now-a-days, no well-arranged play is
complete without something Australian in it, conse-
quently the author introduces us to Mr. Arthur Cuthbert,
who has returned with a portmanteau full of wealth in
the shape of scrip. This is the man who gets into the
toils of the wicked Mrs. Eve Allen and wants very badly
to marry her, and would have done so, no doubt, but for
Sir Charles Hartley, a president of the Royal Academy,
who was once also in love with the lady hut fought shy of
her when he learned her deadly secret, which he uses in
a mean kind of way to break off her match with the
Horsetralian —this secret being that she, when a
married woman, had fled from her husband with a
lover, and had consequently been put through the mill
by Sir James Hannen and George Lewis. At present the
name of the author of this poor play is kept a profound
secret. Some associate the name of VV. S. Gilbert with it,
hut surely he could never have written anything so bad.
Very likely Wyndham is himself the culprit.

Clarissa Stringer, of Melbourne, has leapt
into fame as the enterprising female who implored
two bailiffs with tears in her eyes to give her
half-an-hour in which to “get some assistance
from her friends,” and promptly returned in 17
minutes at the head of 250 unemployed who
chased the bailiffs off the premises and then carted
Clarissa’s furniture to a safe distance, after which
they bombarded the home of the landlord’s agent
with half bricks, and ploughed his front garden
with his iron fence. The police “ quelled the
revolution ” by arresting a small boy named
Moss.
One J. Hewlett Ross writes to the
Australasian :
Friday, the 24th. inst., is the anniversary of the death
of A. L.’Gordon, and, with your permission, I would like
to say to lovers of the poet that I purpose on the follow-
ing Saturday, the 25th. inst., placing upon his grave in
the Brighton Cemetery a wreath of English wild flowers,
which I have brought from England at the special request
of the lady who was, perhaps, the direct cause of Gordon
leaving England as and when he did. Knowing that I
was returning to Melbourne, she gathered and arranged a
wreath of English wild flowers, and asked me to place
them on the grave of one whose memory is still dear to
her, the sweet incense of dear dead days. I will be glad
to meet at the grave, about three in the afternoon, any
admirers of the poet who can he present. There will be
no formal ceremony, but a simple gathering round a
poet’s grave to fulfil the wishes of one he loved.
Only a little simple gathering, the spontaneous
outburst (previously announced in the papers) of
a poetic soul. Only a few faded flowers, and 10 or
12 hulking individuals standing around and
wondering what they came for, and a lonely grave,
and a lop-sided wreath awkwardly jammed on the
tombstone by a big man as he shivers in the winter
blast. And then the ornament will break up as
Hewlett tries to shove it, after the fashion of a
hangman’s knot, over the monument, and he will
probably stand with the ruins in his hand and say
briefly: “This thing’s bust.” And none of the
ten or twelve will have ever seen Gordon, and
very few will have even read him, and all of them
will wonder who Howlett is, and they will all
have a vague idea that there will be a speech, or a
hymn, or some refreshments, or something, and
there won't be anything except the wreath, and a
goat will eat it after they are gone.
Daniel and Thomas Fitzgerald, who are
both natives of Australian soil, have gjod reason
to congratulate themselves upon the ret.alt of their
labours in the very arduous task of placing a pre-
sentable circus exhibition before the public.
Hurry-sknrry-come-qmckly-and-rush-away Ameri-
can catch-penny circuses have “boomed” on the
credulous Australian public and have skipped out
before there was time to properly judge of their
true merits. These tactics were pre-conceived on
the part of those hop-skip-and-jump proprietors
who, however, reckon without their host. The
Fitzgerald Bros.’ circus has come forward as a
genuine revelation of what can be done in an Aus-
tralian arena, and what an Australian equine
manager can do with his horse-flesh. The
Brothers, between them, have run their show upon
amicable arrangements to all parties concerned, and
do not wish to see themselves “left ” in the game
of speculation. “ Dan ” Fitzgerald is 32 years old
and “Tom” 27—good ages for men to be
involved in a risky enterprise, which the
circus business undoubtedly is. They are both
natives of Shellharbour, in the Illawarra district,
a seaport haven which has been the natal locality
of some worthy Australian youths. Naturally,
they look for some recognition from their com-
patriots, therefore it is in no boastful or vaunting
spirit that they submit that their circus is all they
profess it to be, and if they humbly obtrude or re-
iterate the fact that they and all pertaining to
their entourage are Australian, they do so from a
laudable view and hope for encouragement to sup-
port them in their endeavours. They have had
their ups and downs in all conscience, and met
■with disaster in flood and field, but with that
indomitable pluck and energy, ever present in the
native, have come up smiling and conquered un-
toward fate and fortune. Brother “Tom ”is a
vocalist of no mean order, and on one occasion
when the show came to Sydney from the back-
blocks he was engaged at that city for the Sunday
night concerts held in the Criterion Theatre.
The money he received for his professional services
went towards the support of Brother “ Dan ”
and the ponies. Dan was by no means idle
in the interim, as he was busy breaking in trick
horses to be ready for the show when it started
out on the road again. In due course the provinces
were visited, the business done, especially in
Western Australia, being highly gratifying.
Their stable now numbers 70 ponies, all of them
being trained to a degree of excellence, unsur-
passed by any other circus which has toured
Australia. The Bronco horses particularly are
examples of the training abilities and patience
possessed by Mr. Dan Fitzgerald, they having
Been brought to the utmost state of perfec-
tion. Fifty people are engaged in the show,
which goes to prove that the brothers do not
proffer a half-and-half concern to their patrons.
In conclusion, the wish may be expressed that
Messrs. Fitzgerald Brothers will continue to
prosper in their undertaking, for they have un-
doubtedly proved that they have done all that was
possible towards deserving success.

She’s billed no more ’midst Sydney sights ;

How did she cram into those tights?


ANDREW USHER & CO.’S WHISKIES.
In our advertising columns it is intimated that
Messrs. Blake and Riggall, solicitors, Melbourne,
have been appointed attorneys under power to
take proceedings against any person infringing
Messrs. Andrew Usher and Co.’s labels or the
Trade Marks of their whiskies.
The O.V.G. and Special Reserve qualities,
which are the only ones now shipped to this
market, have been so much adulterated by tin-
scrupulous vendors of late, that this warning has
become necessary for the protection of the public.
The proprietors of the brands hold the largest
stocks of whisky in the United Kingdom, and
are therefore justly entitled to the large amount
of popularity they have always enjoyed through-
out the world. Consistency in quality can always
be relied on and to drinkers of this pleasant stimu-
lant, we can confidently recommend ‘Usher’s’
to those who desire a matured spirit.
Health is impossible when the blood is impure,
thick, and sluggish, or when it is thin and im-
poverished. Such conditions give rise to boils,
pimples, and other disorders. Ayer’s Sarsaparilla
purifies and vitalizes the blood.

SYDNEY.
POLITICAL POINTS
Hoyle is obviously glad to have Schey off 1
his back at last.
North Queensland Separation—certainly.
It will create a Republican province.
Parliamentary salaries in Queensland are
nearly certain to be reduced one-half.
If Sir Charles Lilley comes out again it
won’t be as a local politician. Australasian
maybe!
The Australian Republic will own itself.
It will not be in pledge to the Pawnbroker of the
Nations.
Holder’s Ministry is the 40th in the Con-
stitutional history of 8. Australia —40 ministries in
35 years !
There is a growing party in the Queensland
Parliament who favour the disposal of the railways
to an American'syndicate.
Hundreds of Chinese are flooding V estern
Queensland across the S.A. border. In Port
Darwin there are 14,000, mostly destitute, but
ready to follow.
Gladstone, in reply to the Ulster cry that
Home Rule means R.C. ascendancy The
clergy have not become supreme in the R.C.
countries of Europe.”
The trouble between the members of the
Playford Ministry became so strained during the
last fortnight that Attorney-General Homburg
hardly attended a Cabinet meeting.
Ex-Premier Dr. Cockburn, who is an
enthusiastic advocate of the Swiss idea of indi-
vidual election of Ministers by Parliament, intends
to press the subject again this year.
The Victorian Licensed Victuallers’ Asso"
ciation claims that it procured the nomination o 1
no less than 71 candidates at last election, and 50
of them were returned. Government by grog.
The tour Labour members in the Queens-
land Legislative Assembly are known as the four
B’s. They represent Bundamba (Glassey), Burke
(Hoolan), Barc'oo (Ryan) and Bundaberg (Hall).
The present S. A. Parliament is the worst
in history’, and ought to be dissolved. Out of 54
members, 53 are hungry office-seekers, and the
other, being Speaker, reluctantly remains impar-
tial.
Victorian postal estimates of £35,000
reduced to one-half by Treasurer Berry. North
Puddleton can’t have an Elizabethan post-office
with stucco dragons all over it for many a long
day.
Only two votes were affected by nearly 35
hours’ talk in S. A. Assembly office-scramble
debate. The whole of the other members’ votes
were pledged before Holder’s amendment was
tabled,
“ Queensland,” cries Dickson, M.P., “can
only be saved by a reduction of wages and
salaries.” “No,” cry the labour-party, “by a
reduction of incomes—the incomes of land-
owners.”
Bartlett, the jester of S.A. Assembly, has
tabled a motion calling on Surveyor-General
Goyder to resign. He accuses that official of
favouring squatters and other men of cash and
influence.
It is a pitiful sight to see politicians like
Annear, Smyth, Hamilton, and Little, whose
hands are, or ought to be, still brown and hard
with toil, traitorously betraying the class to which
they belong.
The Opposition in the Queensland Parlia-
ment consists of four Labour members—a car-
penter, a shearer and two miners. Two or three
others lend a hand sometimes—the rest are all
Griffilwraithians.
Railway Commissioners Neill and Hill and
Comptroller Pickering each got a son into the
(S.A.) Comptroller’s office recently. In one in-
stance a clerk was reduced in order to make way
for the more favoured youth.
Kingston is great in repartee. Moule, who
it is alleged deserted the Ministry for a portfolio,
said they had deliberately sacrificed principles for
expediency. “ H’m ! ” said Kingston, “ you have
sacrificed principle for interest! ”
'There are rumours of a 10 per cent, all-
round reduction in the pay of the Queensland
Civil Service. This, together with the existing
4 per cent, superannuation fund deduction, makes
the Bananaland C.S. feel far far from gay.
C. L. Garland, ex-M.P. (N.S.W.), writing
to the Sydney press says: “ It is our duty as free
men and lovers of our country, for the sake of the
future of our race, to destroy this abominable
(Queensland slave) traffic even by force of arms.”
Ash has again moved in S.A. Assembly
in favour of striking off customs duties from tea,
coffee, cocoa, kerosene and sugar. He is an un-
compromising Freetrader, and seeks to dish the
Protectionists on the cry for a free breakfa-t-table.
Kingston's speech in the recent S.A. no-
confidence debate was the best of the lot, far and
away. Although bitterly personal at times, the
speech raised Kingston still higher in S.A^
politics. The Premiership is now within easy
distance.
The Victorian Government, the other day,
appointed a committee to draw up a “ scheme of
retrenchment.” Now, the Public Service Board
awake to the fact that this committee virtually
supersedes them, and they are beginning to pack
up. Result—a saving of about .£BOOO a year.
A Griffilwraithiau Bill that tampers with
the Electoral Act is now passing through Parlia-
ment. It places the applicant for a vote absolutely
at the mercy of a police-magistrate, and plays
right into that hireling’s hands when he disallows
the application. In its present form it is simply
a scoundrelly measure.
Each Australian, according to Mulhall,
consumes one gallon and two-tenths of alcohol
per annum. Each Englishmen consumes one
Srllon and nine-tenths per annum. David
hristie Murray should keep his inflamed eye
upon this fact when next he feels inclined to break
loose about the drunkenness of the Australian
people.
“The Protectionist,” says the Otago
(M.L.) Witness in the course of a fearful blast in
favour of Foreign-trade, “is a man who under-
takes to tell Nature what she ought to do.” And
the next paragraph is a eulogistic account of how
the Algerian desert is being turned into a garden
by means of irrigation. If that isn’t telling
Nature what she ought to do—what is ?

tralia, but it goes under the alias of the “ Defence
League.” Johnson, once an impecunious reporter

It is whispered that some of its members have
been putting money into the Conservative daily,
which comes down to a penny next month.
When Graham Berry swept Yictoria with
his Radical host in the late Seventies, he included
in his programme the establishment of a National
Bank. The need of a national note-issuing insti-
tution is greater than ever, but it is hopeless to
look to Berry now for it. Berry is bitten with
the disease of usury, that leaves no man free who
has once sat at the leprous mahogany of a bank
directorate.
Ash, M.L.A., has the reputation of being
the most industrious and conscientious member of
S.A. Assembly. He reads through every Bill
submitted to the House and seldom misses a
“literal” in a parliamentary paper. During his
election campaign he spoke for five hours at a
meeting at East Wellington, and then the greedy
audience passed a resolution asking him to go on
for another hour.
The real question which the enquirer into
the free silver coinage problem must first answer to
his own satisfaction is whether America should
prefer an extension of her relations with external
nations or a development of local resources while
other countries are ignored. Foreign-trade can
only be carried on by means of a arold currency,
but as far as a home-trade is concerned even a
purely paper currency is sufficient.
W. E. Abbott points out that organizer
Stanley, of the Shearers’ Union (now in gaol about
a cheque), narrowly escaped being chosen to run
as a labour-representative for Bourke at last
N.S.W. general election, in company with James
Peter Howe (now in gaol for conspiracy to
defraud), and that had Stanley been so chosen
Bourke would have been represented in Parlia-
ment by two convicted felons out of three
members.
In 1884 “ the King country ” (Maori) was
proclaimed a closed district to the liquor traffic.
A Government Gazette notice has just exempted
“one acre” from this proclamation. That acre
belongs to two half-castes, who mean to start a
grog-shop in the very heart of the country. In
other words, the white government is, in the
interests of greed, deliberately entering upon the
task of sowing the seeds of death among the re-
mainder of the Maori population.
If the Dibbs-Barton Ministry wish to share
with their predecessors the odium of bolstering up
the vampire system of building-society financing,
they will not repeal in the coming session the
Companies’Arrangement Act they rushed through
both Houses in a stupid panic last session. Vic-
toria is bent upon repealing the measure which
suggested the N.S.W. Act, and N.S.W. has
infinitely more reason for following the Southern
province’s example in destroying, than it has for
enacting.
Deakin, having learnt that whites cannot
cope with the summer heats of tropical Australia,
advocates Hindu—not Kanaka—labour, his Indian
experience teaching “that the coolie is cheap,
docile, accustomed to white control, and home-
control, and only accepts employment abroad in
order to earn enough to live comfortably in bis
own village. The coolie would be protected by
joint Indian and Australian legislation.” Another
prophet has arisen ! Kanakilgriff, we know. Be-
hold Hindeakin !
Surely Parkes’ sudden decease may shortly
be expected ! Mr. Dibbs discussing with Baron
Rothschild a proposal to convert all Australian
stocks into 3| per cent. —Mr. Dibbs meeting the
Prince of Wales at a dinner given by Lord Caring-
ton—Mr, Dibbs getting an ovation in the House
of Commons lobby—Mr. Dibbs occupying an
ambassador’s seat at the induction of a Prince to
the House of Lords —Mr. Dibbs dining with Lord
and Lady Leigh, parents of Lady Jersey ! Poor
Parkes ! His boiler will burst!
At the election for North Bundaberg (Q.),
the Kanaka stronghold just carried by Hall, the
Labour candidate, the Labour crowd used to roll
up in force at each of the opposition candidate’s
meetings and yell themselves hoarse in his favour.
They likewise carried motions of confidence
unanimously, and ejected malcontents and
enthused to such an extent that each candidate
believed himself “a moral certainty.” The split
was thus kept up, and on polling day, the good,
guileless proletaire went down to the booth and
vote 1 straight as a die for his own man—and got
him in.
What does the secresy of the ballot amount
to when —as not unfrequently happens in the out-
lying districts of N.S.W, and Queensland—a
polling-booth is opened upon a station with the
owner or manager as returning-officer? When
that official counts the votes, he gets a good,
reliable knowledge of how each man voted, and
then—without in any way interfering with teh
secresy of the ballot—he can promptly sack every
man who didn’t support his candidate. Also, if
the scrutineer is manager of an adjacent station,
he goes and does likewise. The ballot is a great
institution when it is worked properly.
The old Council Chamber, at Camper-
down, Sydney, is now used as an aerated-water
factory. There is a strong hint sticking out here.
Tumkins laid before Tallowfat his claims
to Miss Tallowfat. “Sir!” said Tallowfat, “I
must show you the door.” Tumkins put up his
eyeglass. “Ha ! hum ! it’s a very good door, and
—with proper care—it ought to last you some
years.” He got her.
A boy’s answer to a question in physiology
(from an English paper) :—“The body of a person
is made up to the hed, the thorax and the abdomen.
The hed contains the brains, if ther is any ; the
thorax contains the hart and lungs. The abdomen
contains the bowels, of which ther are five :
a,e,i,o,u, and sometimes w and y.”
“Demos” writes : “ I received letters from
the Secretaries of the Trinity College Library,
Dublin, and the Birmingham Public Library,
asking for certain back numbers of my Imperial
Review, because they were anxious to bind their
sets, but the Melbourne Public Library authorities
do not consider it worth binding.”
The grand old flag of the British 55th
Regiment, which went amissing nearly 100 years
ago, has been found in a pawnshop, the descendant
of the original embezzler having put it up the
spout for a few shillings. This is an omen. Some
of these days the Union Jack will be left with the
national uncle in just the same fashion.
At Bendigo, last week, Judge Williams
went through the hollow mockery of sentencing a
couple of men to death. The first condemned
felon was found guilty of wounding with intent to
murder, and the other man was found guilty of
shooting with intent to kill, the bullets having
stopped short in some bags of groceries that the
“ victim ” carried on his back. In each case the
culprit’s soul was recommended to the Lord for
mercy, although the Lord knew, and the judge
knew the Lord knew that the sentence of death
wouldn’t be put in force. How much longer will
Australia tolerate the existing custom of threaten-
ing to hang (a threat sometimes carried into effect)
alleged would-be murderers for murders that
didn’t occur ? It isn’t Mosaic law, or even
Hinglish law, but simply a remnant of the old,
hideous lag-law, which serves to remind intelligent
natives of the debt they owe to the dear old
Mother Country.
What People Say.
“ Truth is stranger than Fiction,” so people say,
but such truth as we received from T. Channon,
Esq., Photographer, Nowra, N.S.W,, is only a
sample of what comes to hand by every mail from
all parts of Australia. Mr. Channon speaks for
himself : “ I was one of the first attacked bv La
Grippe at Nowra. The symptoms sneezing,
running at nose, weak, watery eyes, frontal head-
ache, as if the brain wanted to press through, the
pain was almost constant, restless sleep, poor
appetite, constipation, foul tongue, constant
expectoration of yellow greenish phlegm, burning
prickling feeling in the chest, great weakness and
pain at the nape of the neck, water—dark brown.
I tried to rise but was too weak, and had to lie
down again. Whilst at the worst, I read of the
curative properties of Clements Tonic, and was
induced to try it, although sceptical about advei'-
tised remedies; after a few doses I certainly felt
better, so much that I wrote you, asking advice,
which you promptly afforded me. I stated my
case and you said it was a severe grip, and recom-
mended a continued course of Clements Tonic. I
followed your advice, and commenced to mend
rapidly and am now entirely recovered. I only
took four bottles, but am continuing it, so as to
thoroughly re-establish my strength (wise man !)
I can recommend it with the greatest con-
fidence for weak and delicate people. Mrs.
Frances Rayner, Oxford-street, Paddington,
N.S.W., also writes, “I have suffered for many
years from dyspepsia and general debility, and
was induced to try Clements Tonic. I have taken
several bottles with such marked success, that I
have every confidence in recommending it to my
friends.” We must again warn the suffering
portion of humanity to be particularly careful that
the packages of Clements Tonic are unbroken and
intact. Several cases have come under our notice
where unscrupulous men have interfered with the
contents of the bottles, and we now place a
guarantee label over every package, refuse all
where this is broken.—F. M. Clements, Melbourne
and Sydney.

Personal Items.
The new S.A. Premier is a Wesleyan local-
preacher.
Explorer Lindsay has gone back to share-
broking again.
The last Lord Mayor of London ate 250
public dinners in 12 months.
Gladstone has large dark-brown eyes with
a kind of yellow rim round them, like those of a
hawk.
Pall Mall Gazette says Miss Murphy
edits Melbourne Punch. There never was a lady
humorist.
Hall, the successful Labour candidate for
Bundaberg (Q.) follows the occupation of a bush-
carpenter.
Steps are being taken to erect a small
memorial over the remains of the late Earoroft
H. Boake.
Mr. De Pentheny O’Kelly is gazetted a
N.S.W. Inspector of Agriculture. “ App'int
Maloney ! ”
Sim. Solomon, the witness whom Deeming
struck in the face while crossing Melbourne gaol-
yard, is dead.
Sir Alfred Stephen has—or had—in his
possession a painting by Thomas Griffiths Wain*
wright, the poisoner-convict.
Phil. May has returned to London, after
about four months’ roving in the south of France
in the interests of the Graphic.
C. 0. Kingston’s friends say you must not
blame him for having a bad temper. His father
was Irish and his mother Portuguese.
They say that Queen Victoria finds fault
with her daughter, the Empress Frederick of Ger-
many, for her nearness in money matters !
There are three Englishmen in the Queens-
land Cabinet and one native (Bow wow !) likewise
a German, a Scotchman, an Irishman, and a
Welshman.
A certain S.A. squatter, who died last
week, leaving a big fortune behind, was so mean
that he made his most recent suit of clothes last
him for three years.
A Sydney curio-collector made an addition
to his gatherings the other day of a genuine rarity.
It was a letter of P ’s, enclosing a cheque in
payment of a tradesman’s account.
Lawyer Gordon, the most active spirit of
the S.A. Opposition, is not above taking favours
from his political opponents, who have just made
his brother a stipendiary magistrate.
N.S.W. Premier Dibbs is “much liked in
England”—confession extorted from the cable-
man. Dibbs, says his enemies, is “a man without
tact.” Exactly—a man without hypocrisy.
The Adelaide twopenny paper always
refers to Precedence Scott as “ Lord Scott.” Con-
servative journals are generally supposed to be
better informed. He isn’t even Great Scott,
Waterloo anniversary round again.
Napier, author of the “Peninsular War,” shut
himself up for a whole day, in mourning, when he
heard Napoleon was dead. Our antagonist is our
helper.
Chief Justice Way is known in Adelaide
as the “long-sentence ” Judge. Way has done all
the hanging cases, neither Boueaut nor Bundey
having yet tried a man who was subsequently
executed.
Mrs. Gordon Baillie, since her release
from prison, has told an interviewer that she
“would like to go and settle in Australia.” There
are many gentlemen here who would like her to
come and “ settle.”
“Of coui’se Canon Dalton, author of the
English Illustrated Magazine article on Prince
George of Wales knows more about him than any-
one else ” writes a soapy critic. And we add “Of
course he tells it all.”
Sir Peter Edlin, the English judge, agaimt
whose harsh decisions London Truth has had so
much to say, has a brother in Adelaide and a
grand-daughter in Melbourne. His sister married
James Payn, the novelist.
The legend goes that the Duke of Genoa
was shown over Victorian Government House by
the proud Chief Secretary, and pointed to the
magnificent Kew Lunatic Asylum in the distance,
with the sarcastic query, “ Do you live there ?”
Many elderly Australians will he pained
to hear that the Duke of Edinburgh weighs nearly
twice as much as he did when we hanged a crank
for shooting at him. His Highness is appallingly
fat, and the heavens begin to tremble when he sits
down.
Reverend Roby Fletcher, Adelaide, some
years ago exchanged the collection - plate for
Broken Hill scrip as less precarious. When t]ie
Lord ran up the price of his shares, he stepped
aside and made a vacancy in the vineyard for a
less worldly-wise parson.
Ernest Faveuc, who is not only one of the
best and most experienced bushmen Australia has
produced, but who also enjoys the distinction of
having developed a greater gift of literary expres-
sion than any other of our. explorers, has in the
press a volume of stories, which are going to be a
big success.
Ex-Chief Secretary Kingston is very anxi-
ous to get back into the good graces of the S.A.
Labour-party, who renounced him when he joined
Playford. If Homburg were not so obstinate,
Charley could have converted his colleagues into
Radicals (or anything else with a good salary)
without any trouble.
Grevin, the great French caricaturist, is
dead. His drawings were so simple that they
seemed to have been done with one stroke of the
pen. His pictures reflected Parisian life, and of
late years, so far as the writer has observe d,
related solely to the adventures of doddering old
men with fast young women.
Postmaster-General Ward boasts that the
present is the “ weightiest ” Ministry Maoriland
has ever had. It averages 15st. 31b. Premier
Ballance over 16st.; Defence and Public Works
Seddon 17st.; Lands MMvenzie 17st. 61b,; Native
Carroll over list. Ward, Education Reeves and
Cadman are the light-weights. Total, 78st.
Friedrich Emil Heinrich Wulff Krichauff,
who now helps business in S.A. Legislative
Council with a crutch, was a member of the first
Legislative Assembly of the province, and even in
those benighted days had pluck enough to advo-
cate payment of members. He used to walk 26
miles each way to attend the sittings of the House.
Lord Deerhurst (our “ Beerthirst ”) is
about to publish a book of adventure in South
Africa. It is not said who wrote it. Beerhurst,
it is expected, will “go for ” Randolph Churchill,
and some spicy extracts from his journal respect-
ing the hero of the lions have appeared in the
daily papers by way of an advance ad. of the
book.
Mrs. Throsby, of Moss Vale (N.S.W.), an
old lady now near her 90th year, is the last sur-
vivor of the massacre of the Boyd at Wangaroa
(M.L.) in 1809. She and three others saved from
the massacre were rescued by old Alexander
Berry, of Shoalhaven (N.S.W ), who adroitly
induced the Maoris to liberate them from the
slavery to which they had been doomed.

These is a spectre in Cromwell House, the
lamented Beaney’s residence in Collins-street,
Melbourne. Beaney’s ghost, so it is averred,
haunts the place at night, and its new tenant, Hr.
O’Hara, is harassed by a cold damp form that
comes along at midnight and proceeds to fill the
bath with invisible water. Evidently the dweller
on the threshold is a clean ghost and washes
regularly.
The well-known English poet and critic,
Andrew Lang, thinks “The Ace, from Snowy
River,” which appeared in The Bulletin’s last
Xmas issue, equal to anything by Whyte Mel vide.
However, Mr. Lang appears to be under the im-
pression that “ The Ace ” was written by the late
Barcroft Boake. This is not so : the author is
“The Banjo,” a well-known young Sydney pro-
fessional man.

Baronet Clarke (says our London corres-
pondent) does not seem to be much sought after
in society this trip. It has leaked out somehow
that he is not quite so rich as he was, and tins
makes an enormous difference in England. He is
generally to be found after dinner in theismoking-
room of the Alexandra, and it is quite pathetic to
see the delight with which Re greets any stray
Australian acquaintance who may drop in. Ihe
poor fellow is quite out of his element away from
Collinsstreet.
R. C. Shearman, ex-police superintendent,
was before Wellington (M.L.) Bankruptcy Court,
the other day. Sir James Prendergast,
K.C.M.G., Chief Justice, Acting Governor, <fec.,
presided. Shearman lost in business his big retir-
ing allowance as'senior-inspector of police. Back
in the fifties, in Maryborough, Vic., a loafer was
being prosecuted. The prosecuting inspector was
named Shearman, resplendent in uniform and
authority. A mild young clerk taking the
depositions was named Prendergast. Clerk and
inspector met again as above.
It is said that Parkes in his forthcoming
“Reminiscences” makes a savage assault on
Thomas Holt, the first N.S.W. Colonial Treasurer
under Constitutional Government. If he does,
the whole story of his feud with Holt will come
out. In his “Murmurs of the Stream,” Parkes
in a footnote, referred to Holt as “ a gentleman
entirely unknown to political life, remarkable for
his crude opinions and infirmities of temper, but
possessing no single qualification for the successful
conduct of public affairs.” The truth was that
Holt was an able but pragmatical man,
A paragraph in Boston (IJ.S.) Sunday
Herald describes the much (self)-advertised A.
G. Hales (“ Smiler ”) as “ a distinguished Austra-
lian journalist, . . . the most widely-known
newspaper man in that distant part of the
earth. . . He is popularly known as the Aus-
tralian Bret Harte. . . He is the Australian
Stanley and Archibald Forbes in one.”' We have
had much reverence for that modest young man
ever since his portrait (representing him in a
velvet coat and Bed ford-cord breeches) was sent to
this office, by some friend of his, for reproduction
on some flimsy pretext or other.
Alfred Deakin is working hard as a plain
bread-and-cheese barrister in Melbourne law-
courts. The voice that used to thunder forth
anathemas at the enemies of Federation and
paint gorgeous word-pictures of the sullen wilder-
ness converted into smiling orchards by the magic
wand of the Water Fairy may now be heard
craving mercy for the trembling penitent who
annexed a pair of boots to feed his starving wife,
or demanding justice for the persecuted Christian
arrested with a crowbar on his person in the back
yard whither he had retired with a view to en-
gaging in silent prayer.
M‘llwraith is a familiar figure in the stalls
of Brisbane Opera House, and when he stalks
heavily in the gods rise as one god and greet him
with a perfect storm of execration. Apparently
he rather enjoys it; whatever his faults may be
the fat, apoplectic Thomas has the pluck of a bag
of devils, and he likes to sit there and hear the
gallery cursing in impotent fury. Griffith do- sn't
visit the theatres much, and when lie does he
keeps out of sight and looks sad and apprehensive.
He belongs to the order of thin, pallid men who
want to run, while his Treasurer is one of the
bulky, red-faced men who always want to stay
and fight.
Some men never die. A week or two ago
the Maori landers were astonished to find old ex-
Fremier Forsaith—he of the Clean Shirt Ministry
—wandering loose about their country, after
having vanished for so many years that he was
generally supposed to be among the ghosts of
ancient history. Forsaith was a merchant, and
was a statesman for 47 hours only. When
the Governor “ sent for 1 ’ him he put on a clean
shirt and went forth and formed a Cabinet. Two
days later the Cabinet burst, and then Forsaith
put on another clean shirt and retired into private
life. He afterwards abandoned trade, and is now
a parson at 'Parramatta (N.S.W.).
“Augur” Chapman, the late sporting
editor of the Australasian, was renowned for his
bad tipping, and yet it was a marvellous tipping
hit that lifted him from the Weekly Times to the
Australasian ; but his constitution was broken
of yore, by working double tides as compositor
and tout. “The ardent young sportsman,” we
read, “used to work until the early hours of the
morning at ‘case,’ and then he would set off on
foot for the training-grounds, where he watched
the doings of the horses, wrote his notes, and
turned up cheerfully again in the even ng in the
composing-room !” There is no eight-hours for
the man who would work himself out of the ruck.
Nobody who ever heard Judge Docker
(N.S.W.) on the bench or in the Anglican Synod,
would give him credit for thinking, much less ex-
pressing, a poetic thought. Yet Henry Kendall
used to quote, with intense appreciation, the con-
cluding lines of the legal versifier’s sonnet to
George Macdonald:
(Words of an unknown tongue and region far),
I lay them at thy feet, their source confessed,
As some dark pool returns with trembling breast
Its broken lights under their parent star.
Six N.S.W. Judges have published poetic screeds
—Barron Field, Stephen (Sir Alfred), Martin,
Francis, Cary, and Docker, and “ Water-Jug '
Foster is strongly suspected of writing a Sunday-
school hymn.
Jemmy Carroll, a half-caste M.P., lias just
been appointed a member of the M.L. Executive,
and is the first person of mixed breed to attain
Cabinet rank. The Honourable Jemmy is remark-
ably handsome, even for a half-caste Maori, and
is a finished orator in both English and Maori.
Before his election to Parliament as member for
one ot the native electorates lie tvas an interpreter
to the House of Representatives. The last native
member of a Maoriland Executive was the Hon.
Hone Nairn (John Nash), who was a make-weight
in Grey’s Administration. Hone had been “caught
young ” by the missionaries, and many a time has
the present writer observed him marching to
church on Sunday with a big “ book-a-book ”
under one arm and his highly-polished boots
under the other. Hone had not mastered the
graces of civilisation sufficiently to appreciate the
value of boots, except for ceremonial purposes.
Some years ago King Tawhiao, who has
permitted himself to be added tu the list of
monarchs retired from the business, in considera-
tion of a pension of <£2so —proceeded to England
for the purpose of laying some grievances
before his great white sister, Kuine Wikitoria
—who said she was unable to see him, though,
scandalous to relate, during the period of his
stay, she contrived to give audience to certain
African niggers who had brought her over
some monkeys as a present. Tawhiao lived,
while in London, at a swell Belgraviau boarding-
house, the proprietress of which, without asking
her tattooed boarder's consent, invited a more or
less distinguished company to meet—so the in-
vitation-card stated —H.R.H. Tawhiao, King of
the Maoris. The old potentate, resenting this
attempt to make a show of him, locked himself up
in his own apartment. The boarding-house
keeper, a lady of much ton , made some
plausible excuse to her disappointed guests, and
the gaieties of the evening were proceeded with.
At about 2 a.m., just before the party broke up,
the old monarch strode into the ball-room, clad
only in a very short shirt, and said to the horrified
hostess: “ Why you no leave match in my
cand’stick ? ” and, having borrowed a few fusees
from Pressman Rathbone (who was present and
who relates the story with much dramatic effect),
solemnly marched off again.
An extremely old woman has just been
sentenced at Orange (N.S. W.) to five years
gaol for arson. She and her husband quar-
relled and'separated 18 years ago, and after
lying quiet all that time the ancient lady suddenly
demanded the furniture of the house her hus-
band lived in, evidently thinking that bare boards
were good enough for the patriarch in his latter
years. Being refused, she announced that she
would “make dust of the old man,” and one night
she tottered round and combusted the cottage, and
went away with a senile cackle as she pictured her
venerable relative with his hair on fire among the
ruins. As it happened, however, the intended
victim simply opened the door and walked out— to
the police-station. The footprints on the damp
ground gave the aged incendiary away, and the
police followed these tracks till they led to her
cottage where she was chuckling dismally beside
an expiring fire;
Even in the early days—as early as ISbO—
there were no fewer than fil applications for a
vacant N.S.W. clerkship—in the Australian
Agricultural Company. The successful candidate,
still living at the advanced age of 83, with health
and faculties unimpaired, is the father of Hon.
11. H. D. White, M.L.C. Born in Ceylon, of an
English father and a Dutch mother, he came from
that island in the same ship with Riddell, Colonial
Secretary. Walking down Pitt-street on the day
of his arrival, he went into the shop of Tawell,
the pious and amorous chemist (the first man
hanged by the agency of the electric telegraph),
who obliged him with the loan of the newspaper
advertising the vacancy, and with the use of pen,
ink, and paper, in a little room at the back of the
shop. A day or two after, William Barton,
accountant to the company, and father of the
present N.S.W. Attorney-General, came down
from Port Stephens, the head quarters of the
company, to see White, who had continued to
lodge on board the vessel after its arrival, and
informed him of his appointment to the vacant
clerkship on account of his recommendations.

It is most convenient to have a first-class
room near tram stopping places, where ladies
can rest themselves, and if they choose, have a
good cnp of tea, or other refreshments. We
understand the Country Milk Co,Cascade,”
80 and 82 Elizabeth street, near King-street, is
just the place ; it is certainly the largest and best-
conducted room in Sydney, and is getting quite
the fashionable resort for afternoon tea. We also
understand that ladies who so desire can have
parcels sent there, so that they can call for them
just before taking the tram for home.*
Use Batho’s Baking Powder.

Society
Various Sydney society girls are each try-
ing to outcry the other over poor little Lord
Ancram’s death. The number of them that he
would have married had he lived is amazing. As
a matter of fact, he was not engaged to any of
them, and his uncle, the Admiral, gave it out
distinctly that he “ would not be allowed to
marry out here.”
* * *
This message was telephoned recently from
Government House, Adelaide, to tho sub editor
of one of the local dailies; —“His Excellency
tlu Admiral desires that in paragraphs referring
to him he should be described either as His Excel-
lency the Admiral or His Excellency Lord Charles
Scott, but not as Admiral Scott or Admiral Lord
Charles Scott.” He is only a vice-admiral, too !
Australia is getting very sick of Ad. Scott.
* * *
Amongst the names of the Ladies’ Grand
Council of the English Primrose League, for this
year, appears that of Mrs. E. Ross-Fairfax, late
of Sydney (don’t forget the hyphen). I won-
der, remarks a Bulletin London correspondent,
how much it cost “ Mister Edward ” to achieve
this honor for his wife. One doesn’t rub shoulders
with Duchesses for nothing, you know. Another
of the Grand Councillors is Mrs. Panmure-Gordon,
who is a daughter of Mr. Hall, formerly of the
firm of Hall and Alderson, tanners, Sydney,
where Mrs. Panmure-Gordon was born. Austra-
lians are coming on. Panmure-Gordon is a stock-
broker, and is reputed to have made nearly a
million sterling out of the promotion of brewery-
companies when the last “ boom ” was on. He
has a house in Carlton Terrace where his wife
entertains largely, principally Jews and city men.
The aristocracy will follow. By the way, Mr.
Robert Lucas-Tanglefoot-Tooth, brewer, of Syd-
ney (don’t forget the hyphens) is also endeavour-
ing to develope into a personage of ton. His
name now begins to appear in the society papers
in connection with the taking by him of shootings
and so on.
• # #
Miss Fanny Campbell, daughter of James
Campbell, M.L.A. (Vic.), is one more well-mean-
ing crank who is going on the road to China to
convert the oily Mongol. If that lady wants a
little exercise before she starts, The Bulletin
would suggest that she should take in the old,
wicked-looking Chow who comes round to her
own back-door with cabbagee, and adopt him as a
son, and convert him if she can. And if she can’t
convert one Chinaman when she has him all to
herself, she may as well give up the idea of turn-
ing herself loose upon 300,000,000 or so of them,
and trying to save them in a heap.
* * *
A certain well-known Sydney usurer pro-
fessed to his clients that he was strong enough to
lend money to all hands with perfect eecresy-he
never re-discounted, so he said. It now tran-
spires that the bills went to the bank every time,
with the Hebrew’s name written on the back in
ink of a sort which could be removed with a wet
sponge.
# * *
The public presumes that the authorities
of a certain Sydney company whose affairs are
now being much talked about will be criminally
prosecuted for issuing a false balance-sheet.
• * #
“You buy cabbagee?” The number of
inmates of Little Bay (Sydney) leper-hospital con-
tinues to increase. There are now 24 under deten-
tion, one of the latest being “a European—a man
not wanting either friends or means, who, having
been informed by his medical advisers that he is
suffering from leprosy, voluntarily gave himself
up to imprisonment in the lazaret.”
• ••*■»
From all The Bulletin hears, the doctors
meet many more cases of leprosy than they report.
• - • •
It is almost safe to predict that quack
practitioners, charged in Sydney with procuring
abortion, will in future be lynched by the juries
before whom they appear, just in the same way as
there is a strong tendency for jurors to lynch the
quack bankers who are now being cut down with
the scythe of the law.

Meeting a certain distinguished actress at
a public assembly the “ dashing ” politician asked
if he might call on her, “ With pleasure, Mr.
,” responded she. “Whin and where?”
then said he in an Irish whisper, audible yards
away. Then she took quite another line. “At
the theatre,” said she—“ you can see me there any
night—from the dress-circle.” *
* # *
“A visit to the lunatic asylums,” writes a
Melbourne correspondent, “ explains many of the
disappearances which have left gaps in Victorian
society since the land-boom. ‘Bolts’ don’t
explain everything. Some of the longest heads
and keenest intellects of Melbourne are now to be
descerned at Kew.”
An eccentric white man of high education
has been living in a state of nature on the Palm
Islands (N.Q.) for years. He discards clothes and
all the other common restraints of civilisation,
and is married to a gin who saved his life. In
town, however, he is a swell of swells. Some-
times he (in a blacks’ canoe) boards a wind-bound
vessel attired in faultless evening-dress and dines.
At present he is in England proving his claim to
a handsome fortune.

It is generally believed that one of . the
late directors of the now notorious Australian
Banking Co., of Sydney, who died with a big
overdraft, committed suicide.
* * *
Apropos “ boy-reporters,” one young man
representing a Sydney evening paper was ordered
out of Central Criminal Court last week during
the hearing of a rape-case. Because he refused to
go, a policeman was told off to assist him. Which
he did.

House “ Scotchiness,’ 1 too.

A Sydney bank-manager has just come in
for about ,£30,000 under the wills of two aged
spinsters whose business he looked after. The
spinsters’ relatives threatened litigation to upset
the wills on the ground of undue influence, but
seemingly there was not evidence to support their
case, for probate to both wills was granted last
week.

Why make such a fuss about tho evanish-
ment of Mrs. Clara Parker ? Everybody knows
in whose interest the lady has disappeared.
# * *
Mrs. Clara Parker’s solicitor told a news-
paper-reporter that the parties who shifted his
interesting client from Melbourne to Albury were
“a professional man and an elderly man.” One
would naturally expect the professional man to be
an elderly man likewise. In fact, no man can be
truly professional until he chews the cud with
dentists’ teeth and his hair is a memory of the
past.
# * *
The case of Barton v. Purves, Q.C., for
restitution of the golden kid, will soon be cropping
up again in a Melbourne law-court. Meanwhile,
a dozen step-fathers of children not more distantly
related to Purves than is the heiress, Kathleen
(who doesn’t seem to be a blood relation in any
degree) are vainly imploring that legal luminary
to carry the little darlings off. A mistaken im-
pression that Purves is an enthusiastic orphan-
collector has got abroad. Men knock at his
front door and press him to accept of impecunious
twins with neglected noses; also, lone widows
endeavour to load him up with their cross eyed
nephews, and the other morning his garden was
invaded by a carroty-headed spinster of tender
years who flopped herself upon a flower-bed and
said she’d come to be adopted by the kind gentle-
man. All of which is very trying for poor Purves.
He is not naturally a Q.C. who takes a burning
interest in other people’s offspring, but he annexed
the seventy-thousand-pounder, Kathleen, because
somebody had made that infant a ward i f Court,
and Purves represents a doosid large percentage of
(Jio Court in his own aggressive person.
# # *
A certain Queensland Supreme Court
Judge collects curios, in the Way of associates.
His latest is a member of the "haw-haw genus,
whose fad is pyjamas. In a fancy pair of these he
shocked the Brisbane bon ton by appearing among
them on the lawn at a fashionable cricket-match
not long ago.
# * *
The Goulburn(N.S.W.) Church of England
people, who for the last ton years or so have been
fighting with the vigour and virulence of
thorough-going bigotry about heaven knows what
(the trouble commenced, though people mostly
don't know it, on the date when a certain dig-
nitary refused his consent to the proposed marriage
between a sucking parson and the girl of his
choice), have elected for their bishop a very mild-
mannered man, with a remarkable power of show-
ing the whites of his ryes, in Rev. Chalmers, of
Brighton, Vie. Mrs. Chalmers, who has a talent
for making peace between people, will be happily
placed at Goulburn. The benign manner in which
the lady acted in the matter of certain floral
wedding decorations at Brighton Church is a
matter of history.

paid by this gentleman, who professed to enrich
his clients —while eluding liis creditors.
* # #
Please remark, that Chalmers, the now
Gpulburn bishop, is just upon the age when the
Victorian Government insists on its Civil servants
retiring. What a satire on the State, —or the
Church ! Which ?
# # *
A well-known Queenslander recently in
England was going to a meeting somewhere in
London. He had a lady with him. As he entered,
two cockneys spotted him as follows : —“ Pipe the
cove, Bill! He’s a Orsetralian. Lor’bless me,
I been there.” The meeting wound up with “God
Save tho Queen.” The fat man from Bananaland
looked disgusted or dyspeptic or something. Any-
how, Bill imagined the former, for he sidled up to
him and said : “Look ’ere, Mister ! I’ve been in
O'rsetralier. If you don’t want to ’art the lady’s
eelings, give me a bob and I’ll ’oot for yer.”

Melbourne evening papers are quite en-
raptured over the gentleness and Christian resig-
nation of the unemployed. The Standard,
gushing about their good behaviour at the new
Labour Bureau, sweetly remarked, “it would do
your heart good to see them.” Awfully nice, don’t
you know!
Here is a queer love-story. Twelve years
ago an intelligent young fellow of 19 entered an
Australian University under the following cir-
cumstances. As a boy he had shown much talent,
and his father, a wealthy, unlettered storekeeper,
decided to make a scholar of him. But the young
man had become enamoured of the family servant
and wished to marry her at once. To this his
father strongly objected, but, finding that opposi-
tion had but little effect upon his son’s resolution,
he effected a compromise : he would consent to
the marriage and give half his fortune to his son,
provided the latter succeeded in gaining the
LL.B. degree. The youth entered upon his
University studies with the greatest ardour, and
in due course obtained his 8.A., and but two
more years’ study was necessary for the L.L.B.
In the second year of his law-reading the object
of his passions, to whom he was still devotedly
attached, married a farmer. The jilted student
fell into a profound melancholy. When this
passed away it left him a monomaniac
with a passion for acquiring degrees. In
this new passion he forgot his girl and her
treachery. lie gained the LL.B. and then set
about becoming Bachelor of Science, which degree
he attained. Then he turned his attention to the
engineering and medical schools and (he was now
8.A., M.A., LL.B., and B.Se.) he saw with
gloomy joy new courses to be conquered, new
degrees to be won. He plodded sturdily through
the engineering course, and at the end of three
more years had the satisfaction of adding B.E. to
his already too-extensive signature. His father
then died and left him in the sole possession of
his wealth, which gave him satisfaction only as a
means whereby he could gain such degrees as
were not conferred at his Alma Mater. He had
still to graduate in Medicine. In the first year of
this course his poor, over-worked mind collapsed.
He became an imbecile with one main delusion
and many minor ones, among which latter was the
idea that he had lost one of his numerous degrees,
lie sought it continually. He was known to have
spent a whole day glaring through a microscope at
some germ-cultures, among which, he averred, his
degree must have dropped. His friends kept him
away from the University in hopes of a recovery,
whereupon he began to haunt the Public Library
and for weeks assiduously studied London Punch.
Then he died.

Defendant in a Melbourne bigamy case
pleads having contracted the second marriage
during a fit of mental weakness. This may be a
novel defence, but it’s nothing new in weddings.

Alexander M‘Kicken kissed a strange
young lady in Flinders-street (Melb.), the other
day, and she gave him in charge. He said that
when he sobered up he was sorry for it, and then

extreme penalty of the law.

Pastor Bevan lectured in Melbourne the
other day on “The Moral Side of Football.” Yet
there is no moral about it, excepting when a book-
maker has three of the best players “up his
sleeve.”

English breach of promise case, that a man may
make a marriage engagement without committing
himself either verbally or by letter. “ A promise
of marriage,” says Wright, “may be made in

Though the babes at home be crying
For a little bit of bread,
And a wife or two be dying,
And a child or two be dead,
It’s a comfort, I declare it,
Though their aspect isn’t gay,
Just to see the men can bear it
In a gentlemanly way.
Yes, of course it’s demmed unpleasant
When a party hasn’t dined,
Even tho’ the man’s a peasant
And his tastes are not refined ;
tint wo fellows—the elect, sir—-
We can stand it well enough
If they starve with due respect, sir,
And don’t cut up beastly rough.
I admire the vulgar masses
When they’re humble, sir, by gad !
And not going on like asses
Bout the rights they never had ;
And when men of strength and stature
Starve with manners, understand,
One has faith in human nature
And feels safe in buying land.
Times for them may yet ire rougher,
Bat “our people ” can endure,
And, however much they suffer,
Property is pretty sure.
'Tis a comfort they don’t pester
Us with threats and actions rash—
This will please the big investor
And the British man of cash.

Auckland Star the other day published a
circumstantial report of the arrival in Auckland
of Colonel Fox, the new Maoriland Commandant,
enlarging on his appearance, giving names of
reception committees, accounts of his inspection
of forts, and all the painful detail. Fox was all
the time hundreds of miles away at Wellington.
Hoax.
# # *
Says G. R. Sims re the scandalous climax
.to the pearl robbery case —“It is not the release
of Mrs. Osborne on the ground of humanity that
is in itself objectionable. It is the fact that the
‘ humanity ’ has not been extended to a woman on
the ground of her womanhood, but to a lady on
the ground of her ladyhood.”
The other day the Brisbane
police arrested a lady with one
meat leg and one wooden
extremity, on a charge of
drunkenness. She was taken to
the lockup in a cab, and on the
way the wooden leg, in some
absolutely unaccountable man-
ner, was lost, and was never
found again. Next morning she
tried to hop into court on one
light, fantastic toe, but she
broke down on the road, and
it being explained that she
had foundered in one of the
corridors and couldn’t get any
further, the bench discharged
her. This was either an act of
kindness or a mean shirking of
-responsibility —it looks very
like the latter. If she had
been brought into court and
then discharged, the bench
would have had to take some
steps in the matter of the lost
leg, for it couldn’t, in common
decency, see a lady quit the
premises on her hands. As it
was however, the magistrate
simply left her on the floor in a
cold passage somewhere, and
the man who would abandon
any female even a drunken
one—in such a strait is deserving
of the reproach of the entire
community.

against the “freaks” of the

the association’s usefulness
seemed to eventuate in a ban-
quet to which Ms.P. and the

vited, and where the blarney-
stone was kissed, swallowed,
and drowned, and mutual back-
scratching indulged in, the
members’ list dwindled down
to the well-paid “hupper hoffi-
cers” who patronised the —haw
—orgies, but who don’t seem
inclined to pay for the liquah.
The Melbourne unemployed marched in
procession at the funeral of the wife of one of
their number one day last week. “There was a
strong detachment of police present, but every-
thing passed off quietly.” Even the poor woman
passed off quietly. The “ strong detachment
would soon have been on hand to arrest her had
she made any demonstration about it. It is a
comfort to know that the brutal mob did not steal
the coffin and eat the hearse-horses.

A certain wealthy Australian lady now
living in London will not allow even Tasmanian
apples on her table. They are vulgar, you know,
and so colonial.

It appears that the marriage of Salmon
Wilson’s daughter with her hard-up earl took
place a month earlier than the date originally
named. Love was impatient, or perhaps the old
father-in-law wouldn’t wait.
« » •
The Victorian Customs people have hit on
a new device for preventing the free entry of
Chinamen on borrowed naturalisation papers. A
Chow who wants to go home and visit the graves
of his ancestors is required to leave behind him in
Melbourne a copy of the photo, attached to his
papers, so that he may be identified on his return.
The efficacy of this check must be apparent to
anyone who tries to tell the difference between
two “Johns” in the flesh. When you come to
take their photos, you find that either is sufficient
to sit for both. In fact, the portraits left at the
Customs are simp’y so many different views of the
same old Chow who dees duty for the entire tribe.
The machine can’t lie, as we are all aware, but it
can’t tell which Chinaman isn’t another one.
Alice same.
The story of how a couple of Melbourne
pressman played the fool with Lily Dampier’s
fallen idol, Watkins, is worth re-telling. That
ponderous young ornament of the drama was
spreading himself out in a restaurant when the
two wags planned their little joke. One of them,
apologising for his temerity in accosting a gentle-
man of such eminence in the purfesh, begged leave
to introduce Mr. “ Watkin Wynne ” to his friend,
a theatrical manager in quest of stars for a pro-
vincial tour. Thereupon the alleged manager
poured out the wildest rot he could string
together and explained that the dream of his life
was to obtain Wynne on a long engagement —at
17s. Gd. per week. Wynne waved his hand scorn-
fully—“l perceive that you are one of those
vampires who suck the blood and brains of un-
fortunate actors,” said he. Then a brilliant idea
occurred to the unsuspecting butt. He would
expose the ignorance of this theatrical manager,
this miserable impostor, by asking him to name,
right there on the spot, the ’birthplace of Shakes-
peare. “Where was Shakespeare born ? ” “ Why,
at Ballarat?” roared the vampire. “Now I know
you to bo an ignorant fellow,” snorted Wynne,
and, folding his arms on his manly breast, the
indignant actor withdrew.

The Metropolitan Board ot Works (Melb.)
has just advertised for two junior clerks, who
must have either matriculated or passed the Civil
Service exam., a certificate of which has to be
produced. This certificate costs a guinea. 1 hey
have further to supply a doctor’s certificate
another half-guinea. The University and the
doctors will reap a nice harvest, as there are hun-
dreds of applications.
A N.S.W. personage who has just succeeded
to a big fortune has a brother in the Liverpool
(N.S.W.) Destitute Asylum to whom, according
to a recent Parliamentary return, he makes the
munificent allowance of one shilling per week.

Bays Parson Chapman, of Melbourne, now
in Palestine; —“There is no such thing as fresh
air in Jerusalem. It is the sacredest, saddest,
and just about the dirtiest city in the world.
Isn’t it about time the churches gave up calling
the Hereafter “ the New Jerusalem ?” If they
called it the new Redfern or the new Colhngwoon
it would sound much more promising.

# # *
The powerful denunciation by Judge
Heydon (N.S.W.), in sentencing the abortionist,
De Wayne, of the class of newspapers which
“ enter into a virtual partnership ” with criminals
of the De Wayne stamp, by inserting their
advertisements, would have had still stronger
point had his Honor been aware that that descrip-
tion of ads. was not inserted except at double
rates. Two of the evening papers, by-the-way,
carefully repeated the Judge’s remarks, but the
other left out the striking allusion to the culpable
press. How considerate that paper is of its
contemporaries !

Coroner Youl, Melbourne, is at work again.
What a fine, lizard phiz., with that round-square
nose, having .a sniff equal to old Judge Moley’s !
Youl likes sensation, something like “Every brick
in the hospital is saturated with the germs of
disease.” He talks with a scalpel.

Two hundred and fifty divorces effected in
Victoria within two years.
During the hearing of a divorce-case in
Melbourne, last week, Ida Honor said that the
evidence was certainly unfit for any decent woman
to hear, and those women who had ally respect for
themselves would leave. All the women present
then vacated their seats and filed out of court —
Joe. J. Simpson, yet extant in Melbourne,
got the big puzzle of his life from G. R r Sims.
Simpson was familiar in Paddy’s Market of yore,
with his melancholy cry “Simpson’s soap!
Simpson’s soap ! Only a few mor—re left, gentle
—men, only a few more left! ” after which he
would break into poetry. Sims, in a yarn, evolved
Simpson and his soap and poetry out of bis
imagination.
Wc sing a sad and simple song
(Behind the office screen)
Concerning nymphs who see no wrong
In anything “ obscene ”
A song that brings a burning blush
To every poet’s face ;
No sympathetic baid would crush
T'io hopes of little dears who rush
To hear a spicy case.
It was the court where judges wise
Unloose the bridal knot —
Win re dimpled Cupid sits and sighs
And shakes bis bond a lot—
Fair women to that calm, retreat
Came flouncing up in force,
For when they want a real treat
There’s nothing in the world to beat
A fairly “ hot ” divorce.
They listened to the tale of sin
With interest intense,
Expecting soon to revel in
“ Disgusting evidence ; ”
Each eye beneath a drooping lid
To sparkle then began ;
Each band in neatly-fitting kid
Wiis slowly raised —“They never did—
He was a shocking man !”
At length His Honor cast around
A solemn, stony glare,
It made him shudder when lie found
Those lovely visions there ;
He saw their nostrils all expand,
He saw their bosoms heave.
He feared the happy angel band
Might suffer in their morals, and
He thought they ought to leave.
A muffled cry of blank dismay
The silence grim awoke ;
Their faces turned an ashen grey,
They couldn’t see the joke
To leave ! when they had just begun
To glory in the sport!
They felt that men have all the fun,
And groaning softly one by one,
They tottered out of court.
How long, how long shall woman cry
To Heaven for her “ rights,”
Whilst fellers wink the other eye
And wallow in delights ?
How long, how long shall matrons dear
And maidens, bless their hearts !
From courts of law be told to clear
As soon as they are getting near
The very naughty parts?
S. M. J. Woods.
S. M. J. Woods - more concisely and popularly known as
“Sammy Woods”—is an example of the nascitur non fit
athlete. Whatever game or sport he puts his hand to, he
becomes proficient at, and he is the only instance I can
call to mind of a man, who has greatly distinguished him-
self at cricket and both kinds of football. He stands six
feet in his socks, weighs fourteen stone, and has excep-
tionally powerful hips and legs, which have no doubt
stood him in good stead in his wear-and-tear style of
bowling. But though his limbs are large, they are not
muscle-bound, as is the case with so many men of striking
physique, and it is undoubtedly elasticity combined with
power which has enabled him to excel in so many fields of
sport.
He was born 24 years ago at Glentield, near Sydney,
N.S.W., son of Mr. John Woods, J.P., of Fanlight, Manly.
In 1884 he came to England and Brighton College,
where he \ ery soon demonstrated his physical capabili-
ties. In the two following years he represented his
school in cricket, football, and fives, and Sussex—his
academic county—in football. Since 1886 he has played
cricket and football for Somersetshire. In 1888 we find
him pursuing his scholastic career as an undergraduate at
Jesus College, Cambridge, at that time a veritable beehive
of athletes. Here in the company of such congenial spirits
as Macgregor, Martin and Mason Scott and Percy Illing-
worth, he contrived to spare sufficient time from his
books to make for himself a reputation as one of the best
all-round athletes that either university has ever turned
out. For four years he represented Cambridge in cricket
and in Eugby football, and it is no exaggeration to say
that, while he was up, the 'Varsity cricket match was
practically a certainty for his side. As a bowler he
possesses any amount of pace and “devil,” and once in
an intercollege match he won the game by bowling seven
wickets for four runs. As a batsman he plays a very
similar game to Lohmann, and if he gets set can rattle up
a score in no time.
For the last throe years he has been one of the most
brilliant forwards in the English fifteen. When on his
mettle and a dry ground, he is as dangerous as any player
of the day in the open. He is fast and powerful, dribbles
at great speed, utilising his Association experience, is
unequalled as a tackier, and a fine place and drop-kick.
The one flaw in his play is that he does not do sufficient
work in the scrimmage—a great pity seeing how useful
a man of his weight and strength ought to he there.
At the Association game he has played for Somerset-
shire in every position of the field, and was in the Jesus
College team which won the Inter-Collegiate Cup tie.
Recently he has taken up golf, and though but a novice,
a few weeks ago won the Burnham monthly gold medal,
astonishing his competitors by some wonderful strokes;
and if he sticks to it will, I am 1 old, become one of the
best golfers in England. His favourite hobby is
cricket and he will tell you that lie is never so happy as
when playing it, but any sort of sport “ has a charm ” for
him. . . , .
At present he lives at Wilstonia, m Somersetshire,
and under the supervision ef Fionde Hancock, an old
International forward, devotes his time to ti e study of
brewing beer, riding, shooting, and following Hi beagles.
Woods has gained the unique distinction of wearing both
the Corinthian and Barbarian caps.

Messrs. Braham and Mutch, of King-
street, Sydney, have notified us that they have
just purchased from the Cellular Clothing
Company the whole of their stock in Australia at
50 per cent, below English invoice, comprising
about 350 doz. singlets and underpants suitable
for winter wear. These goods are well and
favourably known in Europe, although somewhat
new to Australia, and arc strongly recommended
by eminent medical men. They are now on sale
at ridiculously low prices and a sample suit can
be sent through the post on application.
A large and influential gathering of city
gentlemen met one day this week at the Syd-
ney offices of the Volcanic JEration Company,
to witness the new process ot making rerated
waters. . The system, which bids lair to revolu-
tionise the old method, was explained and illus-
trated bv Mr. Wheeler, who holds the patent
rights for N.S.W., and, by its simplicity and
economy, commended itself to all present. A ten-
gallon cylinder of sodawater was made and
charged with carbonic acid gas, at a pressure of
125!bs. to the square-inch, within three minutes,
and was found fully equal to the best brands of
bottled soda now in the market.
Tho gun - accident by which the young
Earl of Ancram, A.D.C. to Governor Jersey, met
bis death on 16th inst., was of the most unlucky
character. The party (consisting of Capt. Leigh
and the deceased, with their host, Mr. Edwards,
of Bibbenluke, his nephew, Mr. Wilbraham
Edwards, and two others), while out shooting,
had halted for lunch. After they had finished,
Lord Anoram and Mr. Edwards, sen., mounted
their horses, and on Mr. Edwards, jnn., proceed-
ing to follow their example, the run of ill-fortune
commenced. To begin with, young Edwards
picked up the wrong gun, a muzzle-loader, which
was charged, instead of his own gun, a breech-
loader, which was empty. Then, as he was mount-
ing, the horse, apparently a nasty brute, rushed in
among the other horses and a scramble ensued in
which the gun went off—probably by striking the
cantle of the saddle. Lord Ancram, hearing the
scuffle, had pulled his horse up a few yards off
and turned in the saddle, and when the gun was
fired he received the contents in the face. Death
must have been instantaneous, but the body sit
upright for 20 seconds, then fell to the ground.
Who says there is no such thing as luck ? What
are the odds against such a thing happening again ?
The picking up of the wrong gun, the horse play-
ing the fool, the gun exploding by accident, the
fact of Lord Ancram stopping instead of riding
on, and the gun being pointed straight at
him, form a train of unlucky circumstances
such as could hardly have been foreseen or
guarded against. The dead man (eldest son and
heir of the Marquis of Lothian) was esteemed as
a courteous and refined gentleman, and his
untimely end much grieves the many friends he
had made in Australia.

Surely it can be said, looking over the annals of
history, and peering through the dim vista of
departed years, that no previous record has ever
been established such as has been obtained by
the Proprietary Medicines manufactured by
Mother Mary Joseph Albert.
His Grace, Archbishop Redwood, the chief
dignitary of the Roman Catholic faith in New
Zealand, lias testified that great benefit has been
derived by persons who have used these remedies.
One of Her Majesty Queen Victoria's most
Honorable Privy Councillors, Lord Onslow,
certifies that he himself has personally taken the
Medicines, and received renewed vigor from their
use ; and now a gentleman (Mr. Walter Bentley),
who has delighted the ears of large audiences in
the principal cities of the Colony, and who has
obtained celebrity as an eminent tragedian, and
one of the brightest stars of his profession, pro-
claims to the world that Mother Mary Joseph
Albert has conferred great benefit on suffering
humanity.
No one would say that gent l emen of such high
social standing would lend their names to any-
thing which was not reliable and genuine, and it
is a fact that these Remedies have proved them-
selves to be so.

PROGRESSION.
THE vegetable medicines of Mother Mary
Joseph Aubert have now become estab-
lished as the favourite remedies, and are the
result of thirty years’ hard study, combined with
the fact that nature, in its beneficence, has pro-
vided an antidote to every indisposition the
human frame is subject to, and which the Rev.
Mo her, in her various researches, has been able
to discover hidden in our New Zealand vegetation.
The preparation known as Natanata is a
specific" for Sickness, Vomiting and Diarrhoea,
three of the most frequent and distressing com-
plaints of females and children. Every housewife
who desires strong and robust health, should take
Natanata at regular intervals, and also give it to
her children. Its action is gentle and certain,
and no one using it can fail to be materially
benefited.
Nature frequently requires assistance, and
when tlie system has been weakened by sickness,
or protracted irregularity, troublesome, or even
fatal results occur, unless counteracted by some
natural and easily assimilated remedy. In sea
sickness, Natanata is simply invaluable, and
those who, on business or pleasure, are travel-
ing, should never he without it.
