The Wild-Cat Column
Castlerag—look, stock and barrel—lately sota at
auctioniforsoino £4OO ! What a ghastly commentary on all
that has been published concerning this mine during the
last two years'. For long it bad a piper run almost
entirely in' its own interests, and a manager who reported
periodically its great and increasing richness. It was
purchased' for £OOOO, one half in shares, when it was a
series of holes in which a miserable lil tie lode of low-grade
galena ore existed. It was praised by ‘‘ experts,” it «as
condemned by practical men whose condemnations were
not published. A -crowd, by unceasing, systematic
booming, got the shares up lo £0 and got out. Then
came the concentrating plant, and after that death and
liquidali >n. A few whose faith in a worthless mine
passeth all understanding, revived it for a second death.
Now, plant purchased for Mqunt Galena; mine aban-
doned.
# # #
Sometimes the local newspaper correspondent
wrestles with the truth in mining matters. According lo
a Tumut wire, Swamp Oak is “looking tip,” and “ there
are 50 claims on pa able gold.” B.vamp Oak is now in
its second year. It lias a number cf reported companies,
several bitteries, has had lo!s cf crushing-and we have
never yet heard of ils paying a dividend. To be told that
there are 50 claims on payable gold is good news—if true.
# # *
Had not an official report on White Rook,
written by one of the few Government mining officials
who know their business, been suppressed in the nick of
time by a somebody who held paid-up shares, a tremend-
ous amount of money would have been saved.
# # #
The big hydraulic sluicing works in connection
with the Spa and Jessop companies on the Shoalhaven,
N.S.W., will be started in a few weeks, and, if successful,
a great movement in sluicing will be inaugurated. Tub
BuUjBTIX predicts success, and with success the washing
away of the old bed of the Shoalhaven river, which con-
sists of oo intless millions of tons of gold-bearing wash-
dirt, more or less payable. It also compliments Mr.
Wilshire, the mining warden, on the persistency with
which ho has, for many years, pushed this big thing
forward on public grounds.
# « *
After all Australia has heard of the wonderful
efficiency of the Luhrig ore-dressing machinery, that
company is apparently not enterprising enough to erect
a plant at their own risk, and other people are not dis-
posed to take it. In view of the great cost of this
machinery, one cannot see anything amazing about its
results. That, too, must be the consensus of opinion in
Victoria, where, if proven results justified it, they would
have the plant erected even if it had to be made of solid
gold, jewelled in 447 holes.
« # #
We have often sympathised with the people of
the Emerald Proprietary mine, because of the sheer
monotony of the reports on the sinking of ButteFs
shaft “a further depth of two-feet-six,” and their
“ expectations to strike emeralds” at any moment. At
last things have changed a bit—two big events have
occurred: “Have had severe weather, so could not get
into the bush;” “Have painted numbers on all the
corner posts of company’s blocks.” We trust this infor-
mation has been cabled to the man gone to London to
float the concern.
» • •
We anticipated as much—the Great Eastern
Lode Cos. did not “go off ” with a wild boom. The pros-
pectus stated that unless 70,000 shares were applied for the
money would tie returned. After a huge amount of
advertising, a big board with a lord at its head, and other
attractions, the public applied for a few hundred, or a few
thousand shares, but the promoters made up the differ-
ence. The days of mine-booming by advt. are over, and
thou'di very late in the day that is some consolation.
* * *
The Glen Elgin mines comprise several gold-
leases near Glen Lines owned by an English company.
They are being well opened up on what looks like a per-
manent quartz-lode. The country is granite-so far,
deeotnnosed. The lode varies from one foot to three
averaging about 18in. Before erecting expensive
machinery the Cos. hired a toy battery and kept it going
at the rate of a ton a day for months testing different
pans of the reef ; the average of free gold was loz sdwts.
per ton. The lode is opened by three tunnels driven
on its course into the hill and by several shafts
sunk to meet the tunnels. Some £3COO have been
very wisely spent in this work to find out what
the' Cos. had to go for. At the foot of the hill,
near the mouth of the bottom tunnel, is the Glen
Ebdn river, an overflowing stream, with which it is in-
tended to drive the battery rock-borers, as well as to
drain and light the mine. The work of bringing in the
water from a higher point up the river is now proceeding.
The mine is well managed, and, to nil appearances, will
recoup the English shareholders, who are spending their
money in the right way.
9 * *
Last year, when Block 10’s were at .£l3, many a
mining man knew that they were working a rich shoot of
ore oMimitrd dimensions, with no great body of payable
ore with which to blend it to a fair, dividend-paying
average. The fact is only discovered, or proclaimed
officially, when the shares have fallen below £5.
# * •
A mass of correspondence has been published re
Baker s Creek Consolidated G.M. Cos., signifying nothing.
H-re is the plain truth about the mine. For twelve
months reports have been glowing and have indicated a
permanent good-sized rich reef. Mouths ago we asked,
“Why don’t they crush ?” there was a big battery idle
close at hand. Now, there turns out to lie only 30 tons
at grass—averaging, says a later report, about an ounce
which will never do, the reef being insignificant. Now
that thousands have been wasted, perhaps the directors
will see they should have amalgamated with the
Baker’s Creek North Cos. which would have saved the
siukinv of anew shaft in the creek—a shaft which has
already been flooded and is always open to this danger.
The D C N. tiave a splendid shaft in a safe position already
down 200 feet below the level of the creek, and from
which the whole of the ground could be worked to the
advantage of both companies. The Cos., moreover, were
always ready to amalgamate for much less than has since
been resulllessly wasted. Because amalgamation did not
suit some people, other people’s money lias been thrown
away. , , *
Remarkable that while one part of the Supreme
Court, in Melbourne, was diving into the transactions of
the Bear Hill “corner” another court was engaged in
hearing a petition for the winding up of that company
because of its inability to meet its liabilities. To success-
fully work a “corner ” in which Vickery and others were
so hnnlv jammed, a worthless mine was worked and
thousands wasted in plant and development. iet many
a New England mining amateur pinned Ins faith and
dropped his money in it, and always asked sympathising
unbelievers “to wait till we crush.” Yes crush, crush-
ing, crushed.
• • *
The Gem Amalgamated mine is, we believe, at
Brimhramulia, but please don’t ask us where that is.
Brimbramulla has got a dandy mining manager, whose
last is that he, with two men, in eleven days, lias raised
18 tons of quartz, “a record which has not been
approached by double the number of men since Brim-
bramulla has been opened.” Then the Brimbramulla man
proceeds to show what he is prepared to do with five
men, five heads and a waterwheel. In fact, says he,
“if the Government were to erect a large battery, 1
guarantee to find good, profitable employment for a thou-
sand of the unemployed.”
* * *
A Melbourne paper tells a story about a mining-
manao-er in the far North. His salary was in arrear, so
I e went fortli in his private capacity and took out a writ
po-iinst himself in ins capacity as legal representative of
the company. Then in the latter capacity he accepted
service of the writ, and said nothing about it to the
i'irectors When the case came on he appeared in his
capacity as plaintiff and proved the debt, but he made no
tmuearance in his capacity as defendant, and judgment
went by default. Then in the one capacity lie issued
execution against himself in the other capacity, and was
iiist coin" off with the company’s office furniture pet
medium of a bailiff when the directors heard of it, and
fired him out in both capacities, and obtained anew
trial.

The Brilliant mine, Charters Towers, has paid
dividends amounting to £3 7s. on its capital value.
The Federal Hydraulic Sluicing Co.’s prospectus
has been issued in Melbourne. Tne property is just across
the border !n Victoria at a spot known as Back Greek, a
few miles from Delegate. The ground, the writer believes
to be good enough, but the very doubtful point about the
concern is the “get away ” for the wash, and on this the
reports of the experts are remarkably silent. With ample
fall for the wash the ground will be worked profitably ;
without, it is worthless.
Webb’s, after many a transition, is now m a
state of liquidation. Although it never declared a divi-
dend the shares once reached £0. One time it puzzled
the management how to treat the ore ; the difficulty over-
come, it was found the ore didn’t contain enough silver
to repay the cost. The show consisted of a small galena
lode of low grade and expensive to smelt at that. Thou-
sands have been spent in the mine and thousands in plant,
and hundreds of thousands in share-gambling.
A CELEBRATED NOVELIST
CHARLES DICKENS.
This clever novelist was by far the most popular
writer that ever lived in England. Living as 1m
did at the same time as Thackeray, he reached a
class of readers whom Thackeray could not touch •
and judged by the mere test of the income derived
from his writings, he stands alone. Shakespeare
is consecrated by two centuries and a half of fame,
and yet as many people buy Dickens as buy
Shakespeare, and for 1 that reads Shakespeare
there are 10 that read Dickens. The truth is that
Dickens had a Shakespeare power of creation
modernised; his characters have a newness, a
vividness, a naturalness that commend them at a
glance to all the world. The best of them, such
as Sam Weller, and that every-day character Mr.
Pecksniff, have more than this; they have that
truthfulness which makes the reader look upon
them not as characters in a novel but as human
beings.
It must be admitted that Dickens was the first
• to bring home with anything like convincing force
to the minds of Londoners the incessant flow of
tragedy and comedy which was going on around
them, and he accomplished this by presenting to
the world his life portraits in simple printer’s ink,
framed in language that could not fail to impress.
His “Oliver Twist’’ did more than a thousand
police reports, aided by the propaganda of all the
charitable organisations of London, to show_ the
tragedy of life in the great city. “Pickwick,”
despite its delightful exaggeration, was true in
showing the absurdities and elements of happy
comedy that everyday life may present us with.
These are, perhaps, the reasons of Dickens’s suc-
cess, and to have succeeded as he did stamps the
man as most remarkable. We need_ not discuss
the question whether his writings will be read a
century hence. But there can be no doubt that
the fame of St. Jacobs Oil will survive even that
of the great novelist, and its praises will be
sounded so long as it continues to rank, as it now
does, as the natural exterminator of rheumatism,
gout, neuralgia, sciatica, and other kindred com-
plaints. For burns, sprains, aches, internal pains,
bruises, cramps, and cuts it stands without an
equal in the world, as thousands upon thousands
daily testify.
A REMARKABLE FORECAST.
Some considerable time since a shrewd financier
declared that the coming watch of the century
would be one that could he sold for a minimum
price; one which should possess the character-
istics of simplicity in construction, interchange-
ableness, and easy replacement of parts, and com-
bining accuracy with convenience in its winding
and setting attachments, and a nicety of- adjust-
ment in its regulation. The period has arrived
when the people are ripe for the introduction of
such a timepiece, and the timepiece itself is here,
answering the forecast in every particular. Each
separate requirement set out is to be found in the
new model short-wind Waterbary watches, series
“K”for gentlemen, and series “N” for ladies.
While the original long-wind Waterbnry, series
“ E,” will never fade out of grateful remembrance
as a faithful friend and infallible recorder of time,
the new series in rolled gold, sterling silver, and
nickel are destined to achieve even a greater
success. They are intended to more fully meet
the ever-growing taste of the public for a time-
keeper which, while possessing all the qualities of
accuracy and durableness that has distinguished
all watches of the Waterbury manufacture, will
also partake in the highest degree of the character
of ornamental appearance, as well as affording
the most approved facilities for winding and
adjustment. Watches of this description, manu-
factured by the celebrated Waterbury Company,
are now offered for sale in all the principal cities
and towns of Australasia. They are finished in
the best style, are chaste in design, embracing
sufficient variety to suit every taste, and it is not
overstepping the mark to say that there is
positively no other timekeeper in the market—
English. American, or Continental—that can in
any respect approach them as inexpensive watches
fit'to he worn by peasant or prince.

Brisbane Telegraph is publishing a story
called “A Living Lie.” Many hug the idea that
it’s a sort of political autobiography.
At Churchdown, Eng., the Gloucester ex-
press lately pulled up because of the signals, and
the signalman was found dead in his box.
“ I was educated to regard liberty as an
evil; I have learned to regard it as a good. That
is a formula which sufficiently explains all the
changes of my political convictions. ’’—Gladstone.
Marshal MacMahon was never celebrated
as a brilliant conversationalist. Onoe he visited
the military school at St. Cyr, and finding a
solitary negro youth among the cadets it occurred
to him that the African must feel lonesome, and
in his own kindly way he tried to encourage him.
“You are a negro,’ he said pleasantly. “Yes,
M. le President.” “ Ah, well! ” said the Marshal,
“ continue so.” And the youth took his advice,
and continued to be a negro indefinitely.
Dear Bulletin, —Those who are about to
try their luck in Western Australia had better
collect a fair amount of cash before doing so.
The only work to be had on the spot is clearing
land for the plough, and cutting sandalwood.
The latter class of work is paid for at £1 per ton.
The novice gets a pound of water-blisters on each
hand to start with, and then retires to the pub. to
discuss coming events ; meanwhile someone grabs
his mortgaged adze and puts him out of work.
Western Australia is a grand place just now—for
Sir John Forrest and his following.— Boodle.
The Otago Workman (Dunedin, M.L.), a
paper which takes a high moral attitude on the
subject of plagiarism, prints on the front page of
its issue of May 28 two unacknowledged Bulletin
articles, with the evident intention of passing
them off as its own. Then a kind friend writes
to Dunedin Globe and evidently tries, in his
own poor way, to do us a good turn, by pointing
out that the articles in question were cadged from
the “Melbourne Bulletin,” a grief-laden comic
print which annexed this paper’s name died of a
broken heart years ago. It is_ pretty hard for
The Bulletin to get credit for its articles, some-
how.
No outsider looking at the N.S. Wales
railway season-ticket fares would guess that the
mother province has any tendency towards demo-
cracy. Take Parramatta, the Sydney suburban
limit; 15 miles. The monthly first-class ticket is
£117s. whilst the yearly ticket is only =£16 2s. 6d.,
so that the poor devil who can only scrape together
sufficient to take out a monthly ticket has to pay
£22 4s. per annum as against Croesus’ £16 2s. 6d.
—a reduction equal to about 27J per cent.
That the handling of £16 2s. 6d. is worth £6 Is.
6d. to the Commissioners is preposterous and
calls aloud to Eddy and Co. to sail under true
colours, and hoist a flag with three golden balls
rampant emblazoned thereon.
The ancient wheeze about the pious soldier
who was saved through a bullet lodging in the
Bible which he carried in his pocket right over his
sympathetic heart, was improved on the other day
by a Wellington (M.L.) evangelist. According
to this man’s story, a private who was serving in
Havelock’s army when it entered Lucknow had
the usual Scripture buttoned inside his waistcoat
and adjacent to his stomach, and while thus
encumbered he was rushed by two mutineers —one
in front with a gun and one on the flank carrying
a large axe. The Briton’s own rifle was empty,
and he felt that it was all up with him. There-
fore he uttered a silent prayer and was going to
lay on with the butt-end, but at that thrilling
moment the man in front fired, and the bullet
glanced off the Bible and killed the person with
the axe, and before the gunner could reload the
British soldier leaped on him and pounded him to
death. This, said the speaker, was another proof
that Providence was a great institution, and the
audience evidently believed him. The average
mission audience, in fact, will believe anything.
Hampered as we are here by cranks,
faddists, and the solvers of every difficulty this
continent possesses—from Federation to the find-
ing of the South Pole—it is cheering to see an
Australian enterprise of great magnitude and
proportions assuming definite shape, and running
on lines so straight as to prove a national benefit.
The prospectus of the New South Wales Refrigera-
ting and Meat Export Company, advertised in our
columns this issue, solves a difficulty successive
Governments have failed toface. The exportation
of surplus stock at a profit has been the want of
N.S. Wales, The Yankee-Irish head of the Darcy
Brothers’ firm has filled the bill to perfection. No
detail, from the establishment of chilling works in
the grazing centres here to the cooling chambers
and “Australian stores ” in London, seems want-
ing. The organising power of another member of
the firm has been utilised successfully in placing
shares amongst the squatters, as the number
of them on the provisional board testifies.
With beef and mutton one shilling per pound in
London and one or two pence here, and the cost
of freezing and carriage so small, it looks equal to
the best gold-mine in Australia, and those who
take shares in this company may find it so.

THIS Company is formed with the object of establishing Refrigerating Works at the various grazing centres of New
South Wales, for the purpose of freezing and exporting meat to the London and other markets, and there to establish
Depots with cooling chambers for its reception and disposal.
The establishment of this Company will supply a long-felt want to the pastoralists of New South Wales, by providing a
market for their vast amount of surplus stock, which is increasing, and is of the most paramount importance to all stock owners
that there should be a market for it without any avoidable delay, so that the rapidly increasing surplus of fat stock should find
a profitable outlet through its agency.
It is to be hoped that all stock owners and others interested in the welfare of the colony will take a practical share in
establishing this Company on a sound and lasting basis, as by so doing they will reap substantial profits, directly and indirectly,
upon their investments, and establish an industry which will prove a great boon to the colony.
No contracts of any kind have been entered into, and the whole of the future arrangements as to the erection of the
Refrigerating Works, etc., are in the hands of the shareholders. The Company will be entirely managed by the shareholders,
who will have the appointment of the Directors and all other officials.
The whole of the capital paid up will be placed to the credit of the Company, less brokerage of five per cent, on the
subscribed capital.
Shares will be allotted in order of priority of application.
Prospectuses, application forms, and all information can be obtained from the Solicitors of the Company, or from Messrs.
D’Arcy Brothers, Brokers, Scott Chambers, 96 Pitt Street, Sydney.
Gentlemen willing to be on the Provisional Board of this Company will please forward their names and addresses in
full to the Brokers.
The Railway Commissioners have written to the Brokers their willingness to supply Refrigerating Cars for the transit
of the frozen meat from the works to the ship’s side.
It is proposed to put works in Yass, Wagga, Hay, Cooma, Forbes or Orange,, Bourke, Tamworth, Deniliquin, Dubbo or
Willington, Newcastle, and such other centres as the shareholders may decide so as to embrace the whole of the colony.
To open Cooling Chambers and Australian Stores in London, there to sell the meat retail.
Lord Carrington is at present advocating in London the support of the Lords and Noblemen of England for Australian
meat, and negotiations are in progress between the brokers and Mr. J. C. Rounding, representing Messrs. Westgarth & Co.,
Colonial Financiers, London, to place half the capital on the London market.
Applications for shares in this Company can be made to the Australian Joint Stock Bank, Sydney, or any of its
Branches in New South Wales; to the Company’s Solicitors, Messrs. Slattery & Heydon, Pitt and Market Streets, Sydney;
or to the Brokers, Messrs. D’Arcy Brothers, Scott Chambers, 96 Pitt-street, Sydney.
All Cheques made payable to the “ New South Wales Refrigerating and Meat -Export Company,” and crossed
A.J.S. Bank.
Ten thousand (10,000) shares were applied for before registration, and 15,000 are already sold:
Wanted Stock and Station Agents in every town in New South Wales to sell shares in the above Company, good
commission. Apply to Messrs . D’ARCY BROTHERS, Brokers,
Scott Chambers, 96) Pitt-street, Sydney.

When the English coal-miners got a rise

What folly it would be to cut grass with a pair of scissors! Yet peo-
ple do equally silly things every day. Modern progress has grown up
from the hooked sickle to the swinging scythe and thence to the lawn
mower. So don’t use scissors!
But do you use SAPOLIO ? If you don’t you are as much behind the
age as if you cut grass with a dinner knife. Once there were no soaps.
Then one soap served all purposes. Now the sensible folks use one soap
in the toilet, another in the tub, one soap in the stables, and SAPOLIO
tor all scouring and house-cleaning.

Bear Bulletin, —Bill Junes, an unem-
ployed on the look-out for a job on a sugar-
plantation, being unsuccessful, was returning dis-
consolately home, but determined as a last resource
to make another trial at a mill in the distance.
He was again told there was no work, when, as he
was leaving, he noticed a breach of the regulations
of the new Polynesian Act: Blacks employed
doing white men’s work in the mill. Gaily he
proceeded to town and laid an information. The
planter was fined—and Bill Jones was summoned
as a trespasser on the plantation, and fined A5, or
three months. Such is law in the Queensland
sugar district.—D.
Dear Bulletin,—J?e Queensland slave-
trade “Government agents.” . Said a “ recruiter’
to me in the good old days : “ The Ariel is an un-
lucky craft. Five times I’ve been aboard her and
something bad has happened. The first time the
captain dies, and we had to put back. The next
trip a hurricane catches us and we become dis-
masted and put back again. The next time the
Government agent has delirium tremens and
orders us back to port. We then get another
Government agent who was tonicked when he
came aboard, and he and the captain have a h
of a drinking bout, and things look desperate.
One morning we hear an awful row, the captain
and all of us rush down to the G.A.’s cabin and
we see him with a razor in either hands jabbing
at his throat, out of which the blood was spurt-
ing yards. The captain tried to rush him, but
he makes a jab at him with the two razors, and we
stood there looking and couldn’t do anything.
When he saw we were frightened he gave a grin,
put his hand to his throat, and tried to pull his
windpipe out and throw it at us. Then he fell
back and died in a minute. That’s the sort of
man who used to go to sea to protect the interests
of the South Sea nigger.”— Disciple.
Neither of the Melbourne evening papers
is particularly prosperous. One of them has had
to ask its preference shareholders to accept a
lower rate of interest than that guaranteed by the
deed of sale; and the other congratulates itself
upon having a large sum—in its shareholders’
pockets — which it can call up when the
bank insists upon the reduction of the over-
draft. But the betting is in favour of the
new-comer since the ancient sinner has passed
under the control of pietists, whose chief aim
is understood to be the salvation of the
staff. The reporters’ book is now headed “ Go
forth in the name of the Lord ” under which
superscription the directions “ City court, Mr.
Simpkins ; ” “ Morgue, Mr. Shrirnpkins ; ”
“Chinese atrocities, Mr. Timpkins;” “Emerald
Hill outrage, Mr. Limpkins,” and other entries of
the kind acquire a godly and religious appearance.
The ultimate intention of the proprietors is to turn
the publication into an extra-reading book for the
little dears in the Methodist Ladies’College, and
Jem Middleton is said to be hard at work upon a
Bowdlerised edition of the adventures of the late
Frederick Bayley Deeming to be circulated among
those innocent young virgh s.

Notice to Artistic and Literary Contributors
Strictly Original matter contributed to Tub Bulletin
W ill he paid for. The Editor will be glad to receive and
consider contributions as under :
1. Original humorous or political matter, illustrated 01
other wise. (Drawings must be made in clean lines
of Indian ink on whitepaper or Bristol-board.)
2. Unpublished anecdotes of living celebrities, or people
who ought to be celebrated.
3. Hunioous poems (from eight to forty-eight lines pre-
ferred).
h. Serious poems, of similar or greater length, cspecialli
those suitable for pictorial illustration.
S. Short Australian or other stories, up to, say, SOW
words. About half that length, or even less, will
be preferred.
All Contributions must be Written Legibly, on One
Side Only of the paper, and folded, not rolled. 2he
name and address of the writer must appear, in all
cases, on the MS. itself, and not mcreb on an accompany-
ing letter or paper.
At Bay.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“I am sorry you have had your ride for nothin",
Mr. Hardington. My husband left home yester-
day, for a fortnight.”
“I didn’t come to see your husband ; I came to
see your dear little self—and Well you know it. ’
he answered, stepping on to the verandah, and
coolly taking the seat to which she had not invited
him.
.. The words struck her like a whip ; in a flash she
saw the depth of the pit her own folly had dug for
her. Folly and vanity—nothing more ; but now
her punishment was come, for there was no mis-
taking the insolent familiarity of this man’s
manner, and, looking at him as he sat there, big,
handsome, and smilint;, she read him at last tor
the heartless villain he was.
“God help me!” she thought; “I meant no evil.”
But she only said, “ I will not pretend to mis-
understand you, sir ! You assume —you dare to
assume!” —flashing into sudden he:it —“that the
terms on which—on which I have been foolish
enough to receive you here —will justify ’ —but
here a keen sense of the indignity overcame her,
and she broke down hopelessly.
“ That’s better !” said Hardington, crossing his
legs, and carelessly tapping his boot with his whip.
“ The tragedy business isn’t in your line a bit, you
know. And all this virtuous indignation is rather
overdone, isn’t it? You’re not a green girl, you
know, and you couldn’t have thought I came
dangling round here for the sake of your hus-
band’s society—little prig that he is 1”
“Mr. Hardington,” she answered, forcing her
voice into a semblance of steadiness, “what I
thought is not to the purpose. What did you
think ? Let me know in plain words. There
must be no misunderstanding about this business !”
Her hard, matter-of-fact tone disconcerted him
a trifle. His cold eye lost a shade of its insolence,
and he laughed uneasily as he answered, “Well,
I thought that, as you had been so kind already !”
and he rose as if to take her hand.
“Not yet!” she said, drawing back, but smiling
as she did so—a smile which puzzled Hardington.
He thought he understood women from top to toe,
but there was something here, he told himself,
which was a trifle beyond him.
“ Sit down,” went on Mrs. Redwood, now out-
wardly as calm as he was. “ Let us begin at the
beginning. There must, as I said, be no mistake
about this matter. Correct me if I am wrong.
You made my acquaintance at the house of a
friend, three months ago, or thereabouts. You
took pains to make yourself agreeable. I found
you well-mannered, well-tempered, and well-
favoured. Pshaw ! sir—why disclaim it? This
is no time for trumpery affectations, and you shall
see that I will be equally candid on my part. I
was pleased with your society—why should I deny
lt; • This bush life is but a dull existence for me,
and any distraction was welcome. Well, you got
into the way of coming here, and you showed—l
don t know what you showed —but you set people
talking An easy matter, you will say, in a little
township like Tarragalla. I know it, and I said,
Let them talk ! Let the galled jade’—but why
hnish the passage for a man whose Shakespeare is
the stud-book ? I meant no harm. Before God, I
say I never had a thought unfaithful to the husband
whom—let me put your purpose into the plain
words you are yourself afraid to give it —the hus-
oancl whom you expect me to dishonour !”
Hardington jumped to his feet, stung, at last,
through his thick hide of self-complacency. A
prettv story, truly !” he said, with a savage sneer.
“You meant no harm! You thought no, evil .
Get people to believe that, if you can, But i
don’t care what you meant or thought. I m not
to be played fast-and-loose with in this way, 1 can
tell you ! I mayn’t know much about Shake-
speare, as you say, but, damn me ! I know how to
bring you off your high horse, at any rate. Your
husband, too ! Come now, Milly,” ho went on,
with a sudden change of tone, “fidelity, and
virtue, and all that are very fine upon paper, we
know, but you are a woman of taste, and it can t
really be that you prefer that insignificant little
rat of a fellow to me ? Why ” —and here he stood
up to his full six-feet-two —“ I could pitch him
over that railing with one hand 1”
“I don’t doubt it,” she sai l. “But, when you
had done so, you would have pitched over a man
immeasurably vour superior in every respect but
one. And, since the solitary advantage which
you have over him is one which any one of your
own bullocks possesses over yourself, he may well
make you a present of it.” . ■ ,
No sarcasm pierces like that which is pointed
With a self-evident truth. Hardington s rage
became white-hot, and his words came slowly and
viciously, as he played his trump-card.
“ You little fool 1” he said. “You are in my
power—body and soul ! \ ou bring your fate upon
yourself. Remember Wandoorah ! ”
Mrs. Redwood looked him steadily in the eyes.
Not a muscle moved, to show the agony of pain
and shame which swept over her at the word. But
her colour faded slowly as she looked, till the cold-
blooded villain, gazing on her ghastly cheek, could
see that this time he had scored indeed. Suddenly
she flashed again. With shame, he thought, but
he was wrong. Shame she felt —bitter and burn-
ing—that this, of all men, should possess her secret.
But it was the flush of a sudden and desperate
resolution that was upon her face as she turned
away and walked steadily intp the house.
“ Wants to pull herself together a bit,’ thought
Hardington. “ I’ve got her, now 1 It was pretty
hard on her. too, but she brought it on herself.
Why, what fresh turn is this ? ”...
She was standing before him again, all traces of
excitement gone from her face. Her cheeks were
pale once more, but her eyes were steady and her
voice was quiet, as she said ; “Sit down, please.
I fear we must have another little explanation.
You mentioned a certain place, just now Wan-
doorah. What of Wandoorah, pray ?
“Oh, you don’t require to be told that ! said
Hardington, sulkily, sitting down again, and look-
ing, as he felt, confounded by her coolness.
“ But I do renuire to be told that,” she said, in
the same unruffled tone. “You have said too
much or too little. What of Wandoorah, please .
I ask you again.” .
“ Well, hang it! if you will have it, you shall.
You lived there, didn’t you ? ”
“ I lived there; yes.'’
“And you made, while you were there, a a
slip, we’ll call it!”
She drooped her head. “ Yes,” she answered,
with a sigh, “I made a slip as you call it. Un,
God !” she went on, as if to herself, that such a
fellow as this should have the power to cast it in
my teeth.” . , ~ ,
“Damn it!” he broke out furiously, do you
presume to take this tone with me? You, who
are ho better than .” ~ ....
“Stop!” she cried, as with a deadly glitter in
her eye she slipped her right hand swiftly into her
pocket. “Don’t use that word! My servant is
gone to the township. There is nobody within
half a mile of us. So, take care I say !’
Hardington stared at her for a moment and
then laughed unaffectedly. “ Why,” he said, is
it actually threatening me? Poor little thing
it must be terribly off its balance. Now, look
here, Milly, let’s have no more of this nonsense.
I’ll put the whole matter in a few words, I know
what happened at Wandoorah. Never mind how
I came by the knowledge. I have all the par-
ticulars, and the long and short of it is that either
you will .”
“Or what 2”
“ Or your husband shall know all.
“Fool! Fool, as well as villain ! My husband
knows it already !” _ ~ , , • „
“ The devil he does !” said Hardington, looking
up at her with a poisonous sneer. “ Well, there s
certainly no aceounting for tastes ! I should have
thought straightlaced Tom Redwood- .
“ Bah !” she interrupted. “You make the
mistake which men of your stamp always make.
Because your wretched little soul cannot estimate
the bitterness of a woman’s repentance, do you
think there is no such thing in the world as a
man? Yes, my husband knows it, but, unlike
you, he has both a heart and a head. And now,
sir, I have but this more to say. Begone ! Forget
you ever heard my name, but do not flatter your-
self that you take away with you my hatred. You
have shown yourself too poor a thing tor hate.
For all your size and strength, for all your broad
shoulders and bearded chin —you are less of a man
than even those poor mutilated creatures to whose
care the Turk consigns his women ! Do you
understand me?” she hissed, almost m his ear as
he sat silenced by the fiery natural eloquence thus
struck out of the woman by her overmastering in-
dignation. _ T .
But he was not beaten yet, _ He got up and
went towards his horse. “lou’re too inuch
excited, now, to know what you’re saying, he
answered, turning round, with the bridle in his
hand. “But I’ll give you this to think over at
your leisure. Your husband, you say, knows all
about you. I daresay he does ; he was always a
soft-hearted fool. But the township doesn t know,
and I swear to you by all that’s holy that, it you
don’t send for me within three days, all 1 arragalla
shall know as much about Mrs. Redwood s past
history as I do. I needn’t tell you it will be all up
a tree with you, then ; but you’ll only have your-
self to thank. You tvould have the straight tip,
you know, and now you’ve got it. Good-day. ’
“Stay!” she cried, running to the top of the
steps as he put his foot in the stirrup. “ Would
you really do what you say ? It would be as bad
as killing me, you understand !”
He hitched up his horse again, and came
towards her. “She’s giving way!’ he thought.
“Yes,” he said aloud, “it would be, as you say,
just as bad as killing you. So, let me hope you 11
hear reason at last. ’"
“ And you would do this to a woman who never
harmed you ; who never harmed a living creature
but herself?” ~ ..
“ Well, it would seem a bit cruel, he said, with
a pitiful snigger. “ But, then, it’s easy for you to
avoid it, you know.” .
“Ay !” she answered, with a sudden setting or
the teeth—“it’s so easy to avoid it, as you say.
And there is only one way, is there? ’ tJ
“ Only one way, and that not a hard one, he
answered, coming closer, but, as he reached for her
hand, it went suddenly into her pocket.
“ You lie, you miserable hound !'’ she cnecl,
“ There is this way 1” and out came her hand with
her husband’s revolver in it. Hardmgton rushed
for his horse, and sprang into the saddle just as a
bullet knocked up a cloud of dust under the
animal’s belly. Again the persecuted woman
fired, as he galloped off, and again—as women
will—she “pulled down,” for the missile extracted
a yell of wrath and pain from the flying Lothario,
as it lodged in his left leg, behind the knee.
Great was the talk at Korumbadgery Station
about the boss’s son having accidentally shot
himself in the leg. Such, at anyrate, was Harding-
ton’s own story —an explanation which did not
altogether commend itself to the surgeon who
dealt with the injured limb. But the surgeon was
a discreet man, not given to asking too many
questions, and thus is it that the crutch which
Hardington will carry to his grave brings him a
commiseration which would be largely discounted
if the true story of his injury were known. But
the man’s vanity is the woman’s safety, and iom
Redwood will never know who discharged the two
chambers of the pistol which he left loaded m all
five.
ALEXANDER MONTGOMERY.

a large sale in the United States. I am willing
to stand by every word in favour of it that I ever
uttered. A man must be fastidious indeed who
is not satisfied with it.”
(Signed) HENRY WARD BEECHER,

The Bank-Manager’s Girl.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Thebe were two men at Bogalong in love with the
one woman, and each of them tried to hide the
fact from the other, because they both worked in
the same bank. It was the only bank in the place
—Bogalong always was a one-horse town—and
Chapson, the manager, and his assistant, Perkins,
formed the whole staff. The woman who was the
cause of the trouble was beautiful, of course. Blue
eyes, flossy hair, and a fine figure on horseback,
Chapson was very much “gone” on her, being
only five-and-twenty, and innocent. Perkins was
“shook,” too —excuse the aboriginal idioms —but
he was a Melbourne man, and “ fly.” Hcr name
was Nelly Jacques.
Miss Jacques, being of age, and having inherited
some property, had a banking-account. Chapson,
merely as an excuse for calling on her—used to
urge her to transfer it from the distant bank with
which she dealt to the Bogalong institution. And
he was quite pleased when she did so, for he fore-
saw many “ business ” interviews between himself
and Miss Jacques looming ahead. Perkins mani-
fested no emotion on the auspicious occasion, how-
ever. He calmly headed a fresh page in the
general ledger, “ Ellen Jacques, Landowner,” and
smiled darkly as the first entry showed “ cr. £lO2
2s. Id.”
Then Miss Jacques’ autographs flowed in freely,
and Chapson used to feel quite proud and almost
benefactor-like as he honoured their demands. In
thi’ee weeks the cr. balance had sunk to £2 odd,
and Chapson was immensely delighted that a
cheque of Nelly’s—ho used to think of her as
“ Nell}’,” now—for £lO should just then be pre-
sented. Of course he paid it at once—what a
capital chance of doing Nelly a little favour !
Then a few more requests to pay bearer tumbled
in (how he hated Perkins’ style of always asking
whether he should pay !) until Miss Jacques’
account stood dr. £25, which Perkins noted with
his usual cold-blooded smile. At this time Chap-
son and Perkins were both courting Miss Jacques
to the very utmost of their respective abilities,
Chapson—poor innocent—thinking himself the
only candidate for the lady’s affections, and the
darksome Perkins lying very low, but well satis-
fied with his chances.
11.
Now came Chapson’s opportunity. Miss Jacques
formally requested from the bank an advance of
£2OO, “ to improve her selection.” Said Chapson :
“Depend upon mo, Miss Jacques, to do the very
best I can for you with the general manager.”
And he received a look of gratitude in reply that
was well worth ten times £2OO, in his opinion,
“ I suppose,” Miss Jacques said, innocently, “I
shall have to sign a mortgage, or a bill-of-sale, or
some other horrid legal paper ?”
Chapson felt quite ashamed of the institution he
represented as he admitted that such would pro-
bably be the case.
“ I’ll let you know by the end of the week, Miss
Nelly,” said he, as he escorted her out, and, under
Perkins’ baleful glare, assisted her on her horse.
“And in the meantime” — tohat an idiot he was —
“any little amounts, you know—er ”
“Mr. Chapson, you’re a perfect dear! You
make this nasty business quite a pleasure. I’m so
much obliged. Goodbye !” And she cantered
away, leaving Chapson in such a joyous state that
he straightway went in and offered to lend Perkins
his horse whenever Perkins liked. Perkins, there-
upon, accepted the offer for the following Sunday,
when he went out riding with Miss Jacques. And
poor old Chapson knew not of it, and was quite
disappointed when he called that Sunday to hear
that “ Miss Nelly was not at home.”
The head office sanctioned the advance to Miss
Jacques, provided that she executed an equitable
mortgage on her land in favour of the bank, and
provided, also, that a competent valuer confirmed
Chapson’s estimate that the said land was worth
£4OO, and Chapson was so far gone that he actually
squared that valuer! Which was necessary, as
will be seen.
But Miss Jacques delayed in the matter of
executing the mortgage —“that stupid lawyer,
Mr. Chapson, is so slow !”—until she had piled up
the respectable debit balance of £9O, and Chapson
began to receive letters weekly from the head-
office inquiring as to “ the security held on Ellen
Jacques’ overdraft,” and reminding him of pre-
vious instructions regarding unsecured advances.
When the amount reached three figures, per-
emptory orders were received from head-quarters
to have the overdraft reduced at once, or sufficient
security lodged. That very day Miss Jacques’
cheque for £BO odd came in with the mail, and
Chapson went round to see her about it. But he
found she had gone away for a month’s holiday.
She had left a letter for him, however, saying she
was so sorry she couldn’t bid him good-bye, that
she would send those parchment things, all pro-
perly signed, next week, and that she was his most
sincerely—
Well, you can’t blame Chapson. What else
could he do? That evening, it became Perkins’
duty, as ledger-keeper, to enter up Miss Jacques
debit as £l9O 3s. 6d. He didn’t care—there was no
responsibility attaching to him—but Chapson was
shaken up a bit next day on receiving a private
letter from a friend at head-quarters to say that
“Ugly Jack” —the very worst of all the bank’s
inspectors —would be round this way during the
week. Also, that he’d better put the screw on all
his unsecured O.D. men, or there’d be squalls !
Now, “ Ugly Jack ” hated Chapson like the very
devil, so Chapson prayed very hard the next few
days for a sight of Miss Jacques or that mortgage
deed. But neither showed up. And the day
before the inspector’s coming arrived.
There was only one way out now. Perkins’
smile was a study that evening, as he entered Up
a credit of .£l9O to Miss Jacques and debited
John Charles Ohapspn’s account with precisely
the same amount on a cheque payable to “self,"
which debit reduced the unhappy manager’s credit
balance to 13s. 9d. But when the inspector came
next day it was very gratifying to Chapson to
note his look of disappointment on viewing Miss
J.’s page. “And I’ll get Nelly to give mo a
cheque for it when she comes back,” he reflected.
“Or maybe there’ll be no need. If she’ll only say
‘yes,’ we’ll—’’ and he gazed into futurity and
was happy. Until Miss Jacques returned.
111.
For she treated him with marked chilliness. In
vain he tried to discover the cause (which was
simply that Perkins had kept Miss Jacques posted
in all the developments of her business at the
bank), uselessly endeavoured to re-assume the
former pleasant relations, and—ass that he was—
brought matters to a climax by asserting that ho
loved Miss Jacques more than anything else in the
world, and would she be his wife? Whereupon
she laughed and said, “Thanks, Mr. Chapson, I’d
rather not! ”
Going home he met Miss Jacques’ lawyer, and
tried to hide his distressed appearance by talking
business. “ Gotthat mortgage ready jet, Pounce?’
said he.
“ That which ? ”
“ Why, you know—Miss Jacques to the bank —
two hundred pounds—land at Dry Creek, with
buildings, tenements, messuages, and so on, and
so forth.”
“First I've heard of it,” said Pounce. “ Say it
again, and say it slow.” So Chapson explained.
“Why, hang it, man, that ground don’t belong
to Miss Jacques. She’s only a quarter interest in
it. And it’s mortgaged up to the handle already,
to the bank at llopefield. Besides, the whole
turn-out ain’t worth more than a .£lOO, at the out-
side, Don’t you go landing any money on that,
my son, or you’ll slip ! ”
I don’t think, for a moment, that it was the loss
of his money that troubled Chapson. What he
fretted over was the girl. And he grew’neglectful
of his work, and failed to flatter and coax folks
into doing business with him, and was surly to
existing customers, and became untidy in his
attire, and fond of whisky and devil’s-pooj. So
business fell off, and Perkins testified strongly
against the manager, and Chapson w’as repri-
manded. This did no good, so he was transferred
to a less important branch, where he played more
pool, and absorbed altogether too much whisky.
When the shortage occurred —a couple of years
later —his friends squared the matter, but Chapson
had to go, and he has since drifted away out of
the ken of all wdio used to know him. The last I
heard of him was that he was a house-to-house tea-
fiend, and there are not many grades below that.
Poor old Jack ! he w*as as good a fellow as ever
lived, but too soft—too soft!
And Miss Jacques ? Oh, she married Perkins.
He’s manager at Bogalong now.
A. CHEE.
Taking His Chance.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
They stood by the door of the Inn on the Rise
May Carney looked up in the bushranger’s eves •
“Oh! why did you come?— it was mad of" you
Jack,
You know chat the troopers are all on your
track.”
A laugh and a shake of his obstinate head—
“l wanted a dance, and I’ll chance it,” he said.
Full twenty-four bushmen had come to tin
“ball,”
But Jack from his youth had been known to them
all, *
And bushmen are soft where a woman is fair,
So the love of May Carney protected him there
And all the short evening—it seems like romance—
She waltzed with a bushranger—taking his chance*
Twas midnight—the dancers stood suddenly still
For hoofs had been heard on the side of the hill!
Ben Duggan, the drover, along the hillside
Came riding as only a bushman can ride.
He sprang from his horse ; to the shanty he sped—
“ The troopers are down in the gully !” he said.
Quite close to the homestead the troopers wer e
seen.
“Clear out and ride hard for the ranges, Jack
Dean.
Be quick!” said May Carney—her hand on hc r
heart—
“ We’ll bluff them awhile, and ’twill give you a
start.”
He lingered a moment-to kiss her, of course—
Then ran to the trees where he’d hobbled hi s
horse.
She ran tdfyhe gate, and the troopers were there—
The jingle of hobbles came faint on the air—
Then loudly she screamed : ’twas only to drown
The treacherous clatter of slip-rails let down.
But troopers are sharp, and she saw at a glance
That someone was taking a desperate chance.
They rode, and they shouted, “Surrender, Jack
Dean ! ”
They called him three times in the name of the
Queen.
Then came from the darkness the clicking of locks,
The crack of the rifles was heard in the rocks !
A shriek and a shout, and a rush of pale men
And there lay the bushranger—chancing it then.
The sergeant dismounted and knelt on the sod
“ Your bushranging’s over—make peace, Jack,
with God ! ”
The bushranger laugh’d—not a word he replied,
But turned to the girl who knelt down by his side
He gazed in her eyes as she lifted his head :
“Just kiss me—my girl—and—l’ll—chance it,” ho
said.
Sydney, Juno, ’92.
HENRY LAWSON.

rangers.

A Prophetic Vision.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
In these days, when psychological subjects are
everywhere being discussed with avidity, and
everywhere we find the educated mind becoming
more tolerant of theories and ideas but lately con-
sidered- extravagant and ridiculous, this story
may be told without compunction.
They were a young and comparatively happy
couple, living in a pleasant Melbourne suburb.
He was spoken of, even by the girls he had flirted
with and deserted, as very handsome. He
was fair and florid —a man without pronounced
characteristics. His wife, on the contrary, was
dark and slight, almost thin, with a hectic flush
and strange eyes—strange not from any peculiarity
of mark or setting, but from the effect produced
upon the beholder by a flash from their depths.
She loved her husband and trusted him.
He was a commercial traveller for a large Mel-
bourne warehouse, and in his bachelor days had
■devoted a good deal of time to barmaids and their
wares, but since his engagement and throughout
his ten months of married life his conduct had
been unexceptionable.
Hitherto his work had been confined to the subur-
ban radius, but the illness of another employe ren-
dered it necessary that he should proceed at short
notice to Hamilton, in the firm’s interest, and the
young couple were parted for the first time since
their wedding.
He went away lightly enough, but she, although
silent, was peculiarly oppressed. The young
woman tried to shake off the presentiments of evil
that shadowed her waking hours and visited her
dreams, but without avail, and on the third even-
ing following her husband’s departure, whilst
sitting by the fire in company with an unmarried
sister, she rose up suddenly after a long silence,
in hysteria, and, pointing with a gesture of frenzy
to a dark part of the room, cried out: —
“ Great Heaven ! It is Jack !” and fell to the
floor in a faint.
Upon recovering, she explained in great agita-
tion that she had seen her husband stretched upon
his back on the ground, in a pool of something
that glistened in the moonlight; his face was
white and rigid, and his eyes stared at the stars.
So strong an impression did this make upon the
mind of the young wife that her faculties were
partially numbed as under the influence of a great
grief. She was sure some terrible evil had
befallen her husband, and refused to be put to bed
or to move from the fireside. She would sit there,
she said, till the news came —she was positive that
it would come, and that soon.
She remained there, tearless, with a white, wan
face turned towards the fire, and spoke never a
word to the sister watching opposite, as the hours
went slowly by.
At length a loud knocking at the door disturbed
the painful silence.
The wife did not move; she barely raised her
eyes.
“You go,” she said. “I could not bear it.
Come back to me at once, and tell me all. Hide
nothing—l am prepared for the worst.”
Her sister rose and passed down the passage to
the street door. She returned in a moment, her
eyes full of tears and her lips trembling.
“They are bringing him here,” she said.
The wife rose, and steadied herself against the
table. Her teeth were set firmly, and her hands
clutched the woodwork at her sides.
Two men came in, slowly—painfully ; they bore
a man’s figure between them, and laid it upon the
floor. This was the form of the husband. There
was. a blood stain upon his face, the clothes were
wet, torn, and muddy.
The terrible vision of the poor young wife had
proved too true. Her husband was dead —dead-
drunk.
E.D.
The Wellington(M.L.)Benevolcnt trustees
send destitute old met) to a subsidised boarding-
house, the inmates whereof, yearning for ham-
and-eggs, hfive just struck against too much break-
fast sausage, and have taken to heaving the whole
lot into the Post newspaper office below. Wild-
eyed reporters are now wallowing in sausage and
saving their slender “screws.”
Ex-Attorncy-General ex-Parson Rutledge,
Queensland, stated recently, at a pious coiVerencc
held in Brisbane, that the awful picture presented
in “ Ctesar’s Column ” would yet be realised “ if
the leavening and sweetening influences of some
such organisations as the Y.M.C.A. were not
pushed on in the woild.” A man like that would
have urged Robespierre to take a drink of ginger-
beer in order to calm himself down and avert the
Revolution.
The first Lord Lytton, we read, had a
strange fondness for personal metamorphoses.
One day he would appear in black , from top to
toe, with a dark-complexioned visage to match.
Another day lie would bo all brown, and on a
third he would be all in white, with blonde hair,
and a fair complexion lighted up with rouge !

Under the heading of “ interesting Items
for Marksmen,” Sydney Echo lays this solemn
fact before its readers : —
A fire occurred recently on one of the moors belonging
to the Duke of Rutland, between the Dove Moore Inn and
Fox House. It is supposed that a spark from a traction
engine hunted the dry bracken and heather, which were
soon fanned into a fierce lire by the strong breeze. Owing
to the very dry state of the fuel, a large tract of moorland
was burned before the fire was extinguished.
Even a little burnt grass 12,000 miles away is
interesting, according to the Sydney Kanaka
organ’s idea, if it belongs to a “Juke.”
Open-air services have been begun by the
English Catholics, after a stoppage of much over
300'years —a very significant move. Tbcjkst was
conducted, quite with state, in Charleston, the
populous Manchester suburb, a few weeks ago, m
connection with Archbishop Vaughan’s translation
from Salford to the Cardinalate of Westminster.
4000 people attended, of all denominations.

To Readers of “The Bulletin.”
“Every man can write at least one book,”every
man with brains has at least one good story to
tell; every man with or without brains moves in
a different circle and knows things unknown to
any other man. Write carefully and plainly on
one side of the paper only, obliterating every un-
necessary word ; then mail your work to Tub
Bulletin, which pays for accepted matter. In
certain of the so-called prize-competitions the
greajt prizes fall to the newspaper-proprietor, who
gets the guileless public to work for him for noth-
ing. Short stories, or ballads, especially on bush,
mining, sporting, social, or dramatic themes, are
preferred by Tub Bulletin ; 1300 words eo to a
column. If you can possibly keep your story
within a column all the bettor Don’t write a
column on any subject if a half-column will do ;
don’t write half-a-eolnmn where a mere paragraph
is enough. “ Boil it down.”
Suggestions for illustration with or without
rough explanatory drawings will ho gladly re-
ceived by Tub Bulletin.
